Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Disputing the Classification: Why Tark al-Haram Does Not Earn Reward
Responding to criticisms of the twelve-category system and clarifying reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that omission of a prohibited act does not automatically earn reward, and evaluate the Mu'tazila claim that punishment for omission of wajib is necessary.

Core Idea
Reward is for positive acts, not mere absence; punishment for omission is not necessarily obligatory.

After establishing the twelve-fold classification of Shar'i rulings, an objection is raised: why not reduce categories by giving each ruling a general meaning covering both act and omission? The answer reveals a deeper usul principle: tark (omission) is a non-act and does not carry the same consequences as positive performance. This leads to a discussion of reward and punishment, distinguishing the Hanafi-Ahl al-Sunna position from the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the passage's opening objection (fa'in qulta). The critic argues that each of the six original categories could be given a general meaning encompassing both commission and omission, thereby reducing the twelve-fold classification back to six. For example, if 'wajib' includes both the act of wajib and the omission of haram (since avoiding haram is wajib), then tark al-haram would fall under 'wajib' and the separate category 'made easy' would be unnecessary.
The objection seems logical at first glance; it aims for simplicity.
2
Understand the Proposed Reduction
Detail the reduction: if 'mandub' includes its omission, then tark makruh tanzihi becomes mandub; if 'mubah' includes both act and omission, it covers all indifferent cases; if 'makruh tanzihi' is general, then omission of mandub becomes makruh tanzihi; similarly, 'makruh tahrimi' would include omission of wajib; and 'haram' would include omission of fard. Thus only six categories (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, haram) would suffice.
This would collapse the careful distinction between act and omission.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi commentator (qultu) replies that the reduction fails because the terms 'rewarded for' (ma yuthab alayh) and 'punished for' (ma yu'aqab alayh) have different referents. For example, the omission of haram (tark al-haram) is not rewarded; only the performance of a wajib act earns reward. Therefore, tark al-haram cannot be placed under 'rewarded for', so it must remain in a separate category (no reward, no punishment). The distinction between act and omission is essential to preserve accuracy in reward and punishment.
The key insight: reward is for doing, not for not doing.
4
Examine the Dispute on Punishment for Omission of Wajib
The passage then clarifies that when the omission of wajib is placed under 'what is punished for', it means deserving punishment potentially, not necessarily. The Mu'tazila hold that Allah must punish every omission of wajib as a matter of rational justice. The Hanafi/Ahl al-Sunna say punishment is not necessarily obligatory (wajib 'ala Allah); Allah may forgive or punish as He wills. Evidence: omission due to forgetfulness or error is not punished, and Quranic verses show Allah's mercy.
This dispute is theological (usul al-din) but impacts usul al-fiqh categorization.
5
Apply to Tark al-Haram: No Automatic Reward
The passage raises a specific problem: the author placed tark al-haram under 'no reward and no punishment'. But since tark al-haram is wajib, shouldn't it earn reward? The answer: reward is for the act of avoidance, not for the mere non-existence of the prohibited act. The Quranic verse 'restraining the soul from desire' praises the act of restraint (a positive action), not the mere absence of sin. Thus, tark al-haram as a non-act does not automatically bring reward; only the positive act of obedience (which may accompany the omission) does.
This preserves the principle that Shar'i rulings apply to acts, not non-existence.

Then, with 'fa'in qulta' an objection was raised: what was the need to consider one side act and the other side omission and make the total categories twelve? Why was it not done such that only the six were relied upon? That is, for example, 'wajib' could have been taken in a general meaning including both act and omission, then in that case the omission of haram would have been included in wajib. Similarly, 'mandub' could also have a general meaning covering act and omission, then the omission of makruh tanzihi would be included in mandub. Similarly, 'mubah' could include both act and omission. 'Makruh karaha tanzihiyya' could also have a general meaning covering act and omission, then the omission of mandub would be included in makruh tanzihi. And 'makruh tahrimi' could also have such generality, then the omission of the terminological wajib would be included in it. And 'haram' could also have generality covering both act and omission, then the omission of fard would be included in haram. So a reduction of categories would be possible in that case, which would be better. Why was this not chosen? The answer (from 'qultu') is: the summary of the answer is that the different meanings of 'what is for it' (ma laha) and 'what is against it' (ma alayha) cannot be included without this detail. For example, when 'wajib' is entered into (the category) 'what is rewarded for' (ma yuthab alayh), then 'wajib' in the general sense, i.e., whether the act of wajib or its omission, the reward for wajib in the sense of non-act of haram cannot enter into 'what is rewarded for'. Therefore, the mentioned detail was necessary. Then it is not hidden: i.e., the omission of wajib which has been entered into (the category) 'what is punished for' (ma yu'aqab alayh), what is meant is deserving punishment. It is not meant that his being punished and tortured will be necessarily obligatory, as is the view of the Mu'tazila. Rather, it is possible, but it will be (by the will of Allah); if Allah forgives, then there will be no punishment, or if the omission of wajib was by forgetfulness or error and was not intentional, then also there is no punishment. In any case, according to the Ahl al-Sunna, punishment for omission of wajib is not necessarily obligatory. 'However, there are investigations (mabahith) appropriate to the context; the first of them is that the author has placed the omission of haram under 'no reward and no punishment'. That is, for this omission there is neither reward nor punishment. Then an objection arises: the omission of haram is wajib, and for wajib reward is earned, and in the Qur'an, upon 'restraining the soul from desire', 'then indeed Paradise is the refuge' is attached. So it is known that the omission of desire, i.e., omission of haram, is a cause of reward and attainment of Paradise. So how is it correct to place it under 'no reward'? The answer: 'The reward is for what is earned' (al-muthab alayh). The summary of the answer is that reward is given for doing a wajib act, not for mere non-commission of haram; i.e., for not doing a haram, reward is not earned. And for every human being at every moment, many desirable things do not (occur?)...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (fa'in qulta) to the twelve-fold classification. The objector argues that it would be simpler to let each category have a general meaning covering both commission and omission, reducing the categories back to six. For example, if 'wajib' is defined generally, it would include the omission of haram (since avoiding haram is obligatory). Similarly, other categories could incorporate their opposites, reducing the system. The commentator responds (qultu) by showing that this reduction is not possible because the categories 'rewarded for' (ma yuthab alayh) and 'punished for' (ma yu'aqab alayh) have distinct referents. For instance, the omission of haram is not rewarded; only the performance of a wajib act earns reward. Therefore, the distinction between act and omission must be maintained. The commentator then clarifies an important point: the punishment for omission (tark) of wajib is not necessarily obligatory according to Ahl al-Sunna, contra the Mu'tazila. Punishment is at Allah's will; it may be forgiven. Finally, a new research (mabhas) begins: the author placed 'tark al-haram' (omission of prohibited) under the category of 'no reward and no punishment'. This is questioned because tark al-haram is wajib and should earn reward. The answer is that reward is for the act of avoiding (which is a positive action), not for the mere non-existence of the act. Thus, mere absence of haram does not automatically merit reward; the presence of an act of avoidance is required. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
ترک (tark): Non-existence of the act ('adam al-fi'l), not restraint of the self.
عقاب (iqaab): Punishment; not necessarily obligatory according to Ahl al-Sunna.
ثواب (thawab): Reward; for doing a wajib act, not for mere non-commission of haram.
حرام (haram): Strictly prohibited; omission of haram is wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi view on reward for omitting a haram act?
A) It always brings reward because it is wajib
B) It does not automatically bring reward because reward is for the act, not mere omission
C) It brings reward only if the omission involves a personal struggle
D) There is no reward or punishment for tark at all

Key Takeaway
Reward is contingent upon positive acts of obedience, not upon the mere absence of sin; similarly, punishment for omission is not necessarily obligatory but is subject to Allah's will.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Redundancy of 'bi al-istidlal' in Defining Fiqh
Critique of the term 'bi al-istidlal' as redundant in fiqh definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that 'bi al-istidlal' is redundant in the definition of fiqh and identify the Hanafi position on the necessity of this qualifier.

Core Idea
Knowledge derived from evidence is necessarily inferential, making 'bi al-istidlal' redundant.

This passage examines a key dispute in the definition of fiqh. The author of the matn added the phrase 'by inference' (bi al-istidlal) to the definition, but the commentator argues it is redundant since knowledge from evidence (min al-adilla) already implies inference. The commentator dismisses the excuses offered in defense of the addition. This dispute highlights the precision required in usul definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Recognize the base definition of fiqh as 'knowledge from evidence' (min al-adilla) and the additional qualifier 'by inference' (bi al-istidlal).
The matn author (Ibn al-Hajib) includes both; the commentator argues the second is superfluous.
2
Understand the commentator's argument
The commentator contends that 'min al-adilla' already necessitates inference because deriving knowledge from evidence requires thoughtful examination (nazar). Thus, 'bi al-istidlal' does not add new meaning.
3
Analyze the rebuttal of excuses
The commentator lists possible excuses (clarification, repelling wahm, etc.) and dismisses them as weak, asserting that the qualifier is simply redundant.
This shows methodological rigor; definitions should not contain unnecessary elements.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position holds that the qualifier is redundant. However, this does not affect the validity of the definition; it is a matter of terminological precision.
Other schools may disagree, but the Hanafi view is based on the nature of istidlal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سے پہلے قول میں کہا ہے و معنی حصول العلم من الدليل انه ينظر فی الدلیل انو تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ علم حاصل من الدلیل وہی ہوتا جہاں نظر فی الدلیل کی ضرورت پیش آئے تو یہ علم استدلالی ہی ہو گا نہ برسی اور اس کے علاوہ ایسے امور میں عموماً اعتبار حیثیات ہوتا ہے یہاں بھی ایسے ہے کہ فقہ ایک ایسا علم ہے جو دلیل سے بحیثیت دلیل ہونے کے حاصل ہو تو جو علم دلیل سے بحیثیت دلیل ہونے کے حاصل ہوگا وہ لا محالہ استدلالی ہو گا لہذا من الادلہ کی قید علم جبرئیل و علم رسول علیہا السلام اور علم مقلد ان تمام کے لئے مخرج ہے لہذا بالاستدلال کی قید لا شک انہ مکرر فان قبل حصول العلم یعنی حصول العلم من الدليل یعنی علم بواسطہ دلیل کے حاصل ہو یہ صاف اور صریح طور پر مشعر ہے کہ یہ علم بالاستدلال ہو گا لہذا علم جبرئیل اور علم رسول علیہ السلام بھی خارج ہو جائے گا اور یہی بات حق ہے لیکن شارح مصنف پر اعتراض کا موقع کبھی نہیں چھوڑتا اس لئے کہا تو سلو یعنی پہلے تو ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ علم حاصل من الدليل لا محالہ استدلالی ہوتا ہے شارح کی جانب سے یہ عدم تسلیم محکم اور سینہ زوری ہے ورنہ تو اہل علم کے نز دیک یہ مسلم ہے کہ جو علم دلیل سے بحیثیت دلیل کے حاصل ہو گا وہ استدلالی ہی ہوتا ہے اور خود شارح نے بھی حصول العلم من الدلیل کے لئے نظر فی الدلیل ضروری قرار دی ہے اور نظر فی الدلیل سے جو علم حاصل ہوگا وہ نظری اور استدلالی ہو گا لا الضروری والبدیسی - فذكر الاستدلال یہ مصنف کے خلاف اپنی عادت پورا کرنے کی خاطر ابن حاجب کی حمایت میں یہ صرف اعذار باردہ ہیں کہ تصریح بما علم التزامنا ہے یا دفع وہم ہے یا یہ قید بیان واقع ہے اور احتراز یہ نہیں ہے یہ تمام اعذار باردہ و ساقط ہیں حق یہی ہے کہ ابن حاجب کی یہ قید مکرر ہے ۔
In a previous statement he said that the meaning of obtaining knowledge from the evidence is that one looks into the evidence. From this it became clear that knowledge obtained from the evidence is only that where the need for looking into the evidence arises; so this knowledge will be inferential, not innate. And besides this, in such matters generally considerations of aspects occur; here too it is the case that fiqh is a knowledge that is obtained from the evidence in the capacity of being evidence. So the knowledge that is obtained from the evidence in the capacity of being evidence will necessarily be inferential. Hence, the condition 'from the evidence' excludes the knowledge of Jibril, the knowledge of the Messenger (peace be upon them), and the knowledge of the muqallid from all of them. Therefore, the condition 'by inference' is undoubtedly redundant. For if it is said: obtaining knowledge from evidence means knowledge through the medium of evidence, this clearly and explicitly indicates that this knowledge will be by inference. Hence, the knowledge of Jibril and the knowledge of the Messenger (peace be upon them) would also be excluded, and this is the truth. But the commentator never misses an opportunity to object to the author. So he said: 'First we do not accept that knowledge obtained from evidence is necessarily inferential.' This non-acceptance on the part of the commentator is stubbornness and overbearing. Otherwise, in the view of people of knowledge, it is accepted that knowledge obtained from evidence in the capacity of evidence is indeed inferential. And the commentator himself has declared looking into the evidence as necessary for obtaining knowledge from evidence, and the knowledge obtained from looking into the evidence will be theoretical and inferential, not necessary and intuitive. So the mention of 'inference' on the part of the author, to fulfill his habit of opposing Ibn al-Hajib, is merely weak excuses: that it is clarification of what is known to be necessary, or repelling a misunderstanding, or that this condition is explanatory, not restrictive. All these excuses are weak and invalid. The truth is that Ibn al-Hajib's condition is redundant.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the definition of fiqh. The author of the matn (presumably Ibn al-Hajib) defined fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their evidences,' and then added 'by inference' (bi al-istidlal). The commentator (Sharh) argues that this addition is redundant because knowledge that is derived from evidence qua evidence is necessarily inferential. The commentator attempts to defend Ibn al-Hajib by suggesting that the condition is meant to emphasize or to exclude certain types of knowledge, but these excuses are dismissed as weak. The true position is that the condition is repetitive. This disagreement illustrates the precision required in defining fiqh and the methodological rigor of Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
حصول العلم من الدليل (husul al-'ilm min al-dalil): Obtaining knowledge from the evidence by looking into it thoughtfully.
بالاستدلال (bi al-istidlal): By inference; a condition added to the definition of fiqh to indicate the method of derivation.
مکرر (mukarrar): Redundant; a term or condition that is repeated unnecessarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the condition 'bi al-istidlal' redundant in the definition of fiqh?
A) Because fiqh is defined as knowledge from evidence, which by nature requires inference.
B) Because the commentator dislikes Ibn al-Hajib.
C) Because the definition originally did not include 'min al-adilla'.
D) Because the condition is meant to include muqallids.

Key Takeaway
The definition of fiqh as knowledge from evidence inherently includes inference, making 'bi al-istidlal' redundant.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Mu'tazila Objections to the Definition of Hukm
Presenting the Mu'tazila's critique that the definition uses an eternal concept for a contingent ruling and includes a word of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mu'tazila objections to the Hanafi definition of hukm and evaluate the ontological and linguistic challenges raised in defining a legal term.

Core Idea
Using an eternal concept (khitab) to define a contingent entity (hukm) is ontologically inconsistent, and the word 'aw' suggests doubt, both undermining the definition.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila critique the standard Hanafi definition of hukm as divine address. They raise two objections: one about the eternal nature of divine speech versus the contingent nature of rulings, and another about the word 'aw' implying doubt. This lesson unpacks those objections and their implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition
Recall the definition: 'The address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the legally responsible individuals by requirement or choice'. This is the target of the critique.
2
Identify the first objection
The Mu'tazila argue that 'khitab' (divine address) is an eternal attribute (qadim) in Hanafi theology, while hukm is a contingent entity (hadith). Defining a contingent thing with an eternal one is logically invalid.
Two proofs are given to establish hukm as hadith: (a) it is described as coming into existence after non-existence, (b) it is caused by contingent causes.
3
Examine the proofs for contingency of hukm
Trace the Mu'tazila's reasoning: hukm is hadith because it is characterized by 'mahsul ba'da al-'adam' (e.g., a woman's permissibility after prohibition) and because it is caused by contingent events (e.g., marriage causing permissibility).
4
Identify the second objection
The Mu'tazila note that the definition contains the word 'aw' (or), which typically implies doubt and uncertainty (tashkik). Since definitions aim to remove ambiguity, including a word that introduces doubt is contradictory.

قوله وقد زاد البعض اعترضت المعتزلة حكم کی تعریف مذکور یعنی خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ المتعلق با فعال المكلفين بالاقتضاء او التخییر پہ معتزلہ نے تین وجوہ سے اعتراض کیا ہے الاول ان الخطاب یعنی خطاب سے مراد کلام نفسی ہے جو تمہارے نزدیک قدیم ہے اور حکم حادث تو حادث کی تعریف میں قدیم کا استعمال کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے كَمَا قِيلَ الحُكْمُ حَادِثٌ وَالخِطَابُ قَدِيمٌ وَلَا يَجُوزُ اسْتِعْمَالُ الْقَدِيمِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الْحَادِثِ فَتَعْرِيفُكُمْ لَيْسَ بِصَحِيحٍ باقی رہا کہ حکم حادث ہے اس پر دو وجوہ سے استدلال کیا گیا ہے۔ اول لكونه متصفا بالمحصول بعد العدم یعنی حکم متصف کیا جاتا ہے حصول بعد العدم کے ساتھ یعنی معدوم تھا اور بعد میں یہ حاصل ہوا اور وجود میں آیا اور جوشی مسبوق بالعدم ہو وہ حادث ہوتی ہے دیکھیئے کہ کہا جاتا ہے حلت المرأة بعد ما لم تكن حلالاً یعنی مرأة حلال نہیں تھی مالم اس کے بعد حلال ہوئی اور حالت حرمت ایک حکم ہے جو بعد العدم حاصل ہو رہا ہے لہذا یہ حادث ہوا دوسری وجہ پیش کرتے ہوئے كَذَلِكَ وَلكونه معللاً بالحادث یعنی معلول بنتا ہے علت حادثہ کا جیسے کہا جاتا ہے حلت بالنكاح وحرمت بالطلاق تو حلت کی نکاح اور حرمت کی طلاق علت ہے یہ دونوں حادث ہیں چونکہ معلول بعد العلہ موجود ہوتا ہے جو امر بعد الحادث وجود میں آئے وہ بطریق اولی حادث ہوتا ہے لہذا حکم حادث ہوا فكيف يَصِحُّ تَعْرِيفُهُ بالقَدِيمِ؟ الثاني انه يشتمل یعنی یه تعريف بالاقتضاء او التخییر كلمة او پر مشتمل ہے جو تشکیک والتردید کے لئے ہوتا ہے یعنی شک و تردد ظاہر کرنے کے لئے یا دوسرے کو شک و تردید میں مبتلا کرنے کے لئے آتا ہے یہ تعریف کے منافی ہے کیونکہ تعریف سے مقصود معرفت معرف ہوتی ہے اور تشکیک و تردید ابہام کے لئے ہوتے ہیں جو معرفت اور تعریف کے منافی ہیں۔
His statement: 'And some added' — the Mu'tazila objected to the mentioned definition of hukm, i.e., 'the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the legally responsible individuals by requirement or choice', from three aspects. First: that 'al-khitab' means the inner speech (kalam nafsi) which, according to you, is eternal (qadim), while hukm is contingent (hadith). So using the eternal in the definition of the contingent is not correct. As it is said: 'Hukm is contingent, khitab is eternal, and it is not permissible to use the eternal in the definition of the contingent.' So your definition is not valid. It remains to be proven that hukm is contingent. It is argued by two proofs: First, because it is described as coming into existence after non-existence (al-mahsul ba'da al-'adam). Hukm is described as being obtained after non-existence, i.e., it was non-existent and then came into existence. Whatever is preceded by non-existence is contingent. For example, it is said: 'She became permissible after she was not permissible.' The state of prohibition is a hukm that comes into existence after non-existence, thus it is contingent. Second, because it is caused by a contingent cause (mu'allal bil hadith). For example, it is said: 'She became permissible by marriage and prohibited by divorce.' Marriage as a cause for permissibility and divorce for prohibition are both contingent. Since the effect comes after the cause, whatever comes into existence after a contingent event is a fortiori contingent. Hence hukm is contingent. So how can it be valid to define it with the eternal? Second (objection): that this definition (by requirement or choice) includes the word 'aw' (or), which is used for doubt and uncertainty (tashkik wa al-taraddud), i.e., to show doubt or to put someone in doubt. This is contrary to definition, because the purpose of definition is to make the defined known (ta'rif), while doubt and uncertainty cause ambiguity, which is contrary to knowledge and definition.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents two objections of the Mu'tazila against the Hanafi definition of hukm. The Mu'tazila raise three objections (though only two are detailed in this portion). First, the definition uses 'khitab' (address) which according to Hanafis is the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi), while hukm (legal ruling) is contingent (hadith) because it comes into existence after non-existence and is caused by contingent causes like marriage or divorce. Defining a contingent entity with an eternal one is logically invalid. The Hanafi response is not provided in this text. Second, the definition contains the word 'aw' (or) which implies doubt and hesitation, whereas a proper definition should remove ambiguity, not create it. The Mu'tazila argue that the disjunctive 'or' suggests uncertainty, making the definition unsuitable. The passage stops short of the third objection and does not include the Hanafi rebuttal, but it faithfully records the Mu'tazila critique.

Key Terms
قدیم (qadim): That which has no beginning; eternal. Used here for the inner speech of Allah (kalam nafsi).
حادث (hadith): That which comes into existence after non-existence; contingent. Used here for hukm (legal ruling).
او (aw): The Arabic word 'or' which, according to the Mu'tazila, implies doubt and uncertainty (tashkik) in the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why is the Hanafi definition of hukm (as 'khitab Allah... bi al-iqtida aw al-takhyir') invalid?
A) Because it does not cover permissibility.
B) Because it uses an eternal entity (khitab) to define a contingent entity (hukm).
C) Because it does not include the Prophet's commands.
D) Because it repeats the word 'mukallafin'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objections highlight the importance of ontological consistency and linguistic precision in legal definitions in usul al-fiqh.

Defending the Definition of Fiqh: The 'Amaliyya Condition and Exclusion of Moral Qualities
Refuting the Ash'ari objection regarding the inclusion of moral qualities in fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of fiqh and apply the 'amaliyya condition to distinguish fiqh from ethics, and evaluate the Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
Fiqh is only about practical actions ('amaliyya), not inner morals – the 'amaliyya condition excludes ethics.

In this lesson, we examine a key dispute in the definition of fiqh. The Hanafis define fiqh as knowledge of practical shar'i rulings, adding the condition 'amaliyya (practical). The Ash'aris object that this definition still includes moral qualities like generosity and humility because they are shar'i rulings known through revelation. The Hanafi response relies on the 'amaliyya condition to exclude these inner dispositions, arguing that fiqh deals only with external actions of the limbs. We will explore the logic behind this condition and how it preserves the boundary between fiqh and ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition of fiqh and its key conditions
We recall that the standard Hanafi definition is 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence.' The word 'practical' (ʿamaliyya) is crucial. Note that it restricts the scope to actions of the limbs.
The condition of ʿamaliyya is unique to Hanafi usul.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris argue that moral qualities like humility and generosity are shar'i rulings (good/evil known only through revelation). If fiqh is knowledge of shar'i rulings, then knowledge of these moral qualities would also be fiqh. However, they are actually part of ethics, not fiqh. So the Hanafi definition fails to prevent the entry of non-fiqh matters.
This is the objection the author raises.
3
State the Hanafi response using the 'amaliyya condition
The Hanafi commentator argues that the 'amaliyya condition explicitly excludes inner moral qualities because they are not practical actions of the limbs. Even if they are shar'i rulings, they are not ʿamaliyya. Thus, they remain outside fiqh.
The condition acts as a preventer (maniʿ) against ethics entering fiqh.
4
Address the subtlety about external effects of moral qualities
The passage discusses that even the outward effects of moral qualities (e.g., stroking an orphan's head) are practical actions but are not part of terminological fiqh. The commentator concludes that the author did not forget the condition; rather, the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the scope of fiqh.
This shows the precision of Hanafi usul in defining boundaries.

مذکور ہے یہ بھی تو از قسم عمل ہے لیکن فقہ کے مباحث میں درج نہیں ہے اس قسم کے اعمال تعریف فقہ میں داخل ہو جائیں گے لیکن فقہ اصطلاحی نہیں ہیں تو منعیت تعریف منتقض ہو جائے گی واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
There is no intellectual good or evil, so according to them [Ash'aris] reason has no scope or power in understanding rulings. Thus knowledge of rulings cannot be without the Shari'a; rather all rulings depend on the Shari'a. Those rulings with which a Shari'a address comes are hukm; where the address of the Shari'a is not present, in the view of the Ash'aris there is no ruling. Hence according to the Ash'aris, rulings are what are not known except through the address of the Lawgiver. So if the address of Allah is a definition of the absolute Wise, and not of a Shari'a ruling, then in the definition of fiqh the restriction 'shar'iyya' towards the rulings would necessarily lead to repetition whether they interpret shar'iyya as what comes with the address of the Shari'a or what is not known except through the address of the Lawgiver. His statement 'then it includes' means that the definition of fiqh: this is the same objection that the author made against the Ash'aris, that this definition of fiqh does not prevent the entry of what is not [fiqh]. The summary is that the goodness of humility and goodness of generosity—i.e., their obligatoriness or recommendation—and similarly the evil of stinginess and arrogance—i.e., their prohibition or dislike—and similar moral qualities according to the Ash'aris would be included in fiqh, and the definition of fiqh would encompass them, when knowledge of them is obtained from evidence, because according to the Ash'aris these are such rulings that their perception cannot be without the Shari'a, since in their view reason has no role in perceiving rulings. Yet these are part of ethics, not fiqh. Hence in their opinion this definition does not prevent the entry of what is not [fiqh]. I say: The only binding consequence would be if these kinds of rulings were practical (muhmaliyya) in the aforementioned sense, meaning they relate to actions of the limbs, which is not the case. These rulings are not practical; rather the mentioned matters are morals and dispositions of the soul. And the author has already designated knowledge of their goodness and evil as ethics, and has clearly stated in the preceding that in the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the self of what is for it and against it', the condition of 'amalī is added so that all ethics are excluded. And he has also clarified that 'knowledge of the self of what is for it and against it' is divided into two categories: experiential (wijdāniyyāt), i.e., the inner morals and dispositions of the soul, which are called ethics; and the practical (ʿamaliyyāt), which are called fiqh. So since in the mentioned definition of fiqh, along with shar'iyya rulings, the condition of 'amaliyya is present, even if goodness of generosity and humility are according to the Ash'aris included in shar'iyya rulings, the condition of 'amaliyya excludes them from the definition of fiqh. So it seems that the author, who earlier presented the benefit of the condition of 'amaliyya, has forgotten it here, or he has neglected the condition of 'amaliyya present in the definition. It is said: The author did not forget, nor was he neglectful; rather the commentator did not ponder the author's words. Because the author's intention by 'generosity and humility' is not the inner morals and dispositions of the soul, but their outward effects, which are of the category of actions of the limbs. For example, practically showing humility, practically showing sympathy and affection to the poor and orphan, and comforting and consoling them verbally—all these are practical. But they are not included in fiqh. However, according to the Ash'aris, all these are shar'iyya-ʿamaliyya rulings, so they enter fiqh, thus the definition is no longer preventative. Although among the effects of generosity, giving financial charity is included in fiqh, but such sympathetic physical actions—these too are effects of those dispositions, which fiqh does not discuss. As in the noble hadith, the virtue of placing a merciful hand on the orphan's head is mentioned; this too is a type of action, but it is not included in the discussions of fiqh. Such actions would enter the definition of fiqh, but they are not terminological fiqh, so the preventiveness of the definition would be violated. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of fiqh, addressing an objection raised by the author (presumably the author of Al-Tawdih) against the Ash'ari school. The Ash'aris hold that moral qualities like generosity and humility (which they consider wajib or mandub) are shar'i rulings, and since they are perceived only through revelation (not reason), they would fall under the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence.' The commentator explains that the condition of 'amaliyya (practical) in the Hanafi definition excludes these inner moral qualities, because fiqh deals with external actions of the limbs, not internal states. However, the outward effects of these qualities (e.g., showing sympathy to an orphan) are external actions, but they are not part of terminological fiqh; they belong to ethics. The passage also debates whether the author himself forgot the 'amaliyya condition, concluding that he did not; rather, his mention of generosity and humility referred to their external effects, which though practical, are not discussed in fiqh (like stroking an orphan's head). Thus the definition remains preventive. The passage demonstrates the precision of Hanafi usul in distinguishing fiqh from ethics.

Key Terms
حکم شرعی (ḥukm sharʿī): A ruling from the Lawgiver concerning the actions of the responsible person.
عملیہ (ʿamaliyya): Practical; relating to external actions of the body.
فقہ (fiqh): Knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition of fiqh, why are moral qualities like generosity excluded from fiqh?
A) Because they are not shar'i rulings.
B) Because the definition includes the condition 'amaliyya (practical), and moral qualities are inward, not practical actions.
C) Because they are known through reason, not revelation.
D) Because they are not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The 'amaliyya condition in the Hanafi definition of fiqh ensures that only practical, limb-based rulings are included, thereby excluding inner moral qualities and preserving a clear boundary between fiqh and ethics.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Shafi'i Condition of Iktisab in Defining Fiqh
Explaining the Shafi'i condition of iktisab in defining fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Shafi'i condition of iktisab in the definition of fiqh and distinguish it from the Hanafi definition.

Core Idea
Shafi'is require fiqh to be acquired through ijtihad, excluding self-evident knowledge.

This passage clarifies why the Shafi'is add the condition of iktisab (acquisition through reflection) to the definition of fiqh. It explains that this condition excludes knowledge of matters known necessarily from religion, such as the obligation of prayer, and addresses a common objection that the condition is unnecessary. Understanding this subtle point deepens your grasp of how different schools define the very discipline of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i condition
Locate where the text states that Shafi'is require fiqh to be acquired through nazar (reflection) and istidlal (inference). Note the Arabic terms: اکتساب and استدلال.
This is the core addition to the definition.
2
Understand what the condition excludes
The text says iktisab excludes badihiyyat (self-evident knowledge), like the obligation of prayer, because such knowledge is not acquired through ijtihad. Identify the two categories excluded: badihiyyat and knowledge of usul principles (like ijma being a proof).
The condition ensures fiqh is only knowledge that required personal reasoning.
3
Analyze the author's objection and the clarification
The author (Sadr al-Shariah) objected that the condition would wrongly imply that someone knowing only obvious rulings is not a faqih. The commentator clarifies that the condition is not to avoid that implication; rather, fiqh itself implies comprehensive knowledge. Even without the condition, one who knows only a few rulings is not called a faqih.
This shows that the Shafi'i condition serves a different purpose: excluding knowledge that lacks ijtihad, not limiting the quantity of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولا يزاد عليه المصطلح بين الشافعية ا یعنی شوافع کی اصطلاح یہ ہے کہ علم بالا حکام کو فقہ کے نام کے ساتھ اس وقت موسوم کیا جائے گا جب اس علم کا حصول بطریق نظر و استدلال ہو حتی کہ علم بوجوب الصلوة والصوم وغیرہ جن کا دین میں سے ہونا مشہور اور بدیہی ہے جس کو متدین و غیر متدین جانتا ہے ان کے علم کو شوافع کے نزدیک فقہ اصطلاحی سے شمار نہیں کیا جاتا اسی وجہ سے یہ حضرات تعریف فقہ میں قید اکتساب یا استدلال ذکر کرتے ہیں امام رازی نے محصول میں التی لا یعلم کو نہا من الدین بالضرورۃ کی قید لگائی اور کہا کہ یہ علم بوجوب الصلواۃ والصوم سے احتراز ہے کہ ان کے علم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا بمعنى انه لا يدخل في مسمى الفقه یعنی یہ علم مسمی الفقہ میں اصطلا حا داخل نہیں اور ان کو فقہ سے شمار نہیں کیا جاتا على ما صرح به في قيد العملية جیسے قید عملیہ میں رازی نے تصریح کی کہ یہ علم بكون الاجماع والقیاس و خبر الواحد حجہ سے احتراز ہے باوجود اس کے کہ یہ احکام شرعیہ تو ہیں لیکن ان احکام کے علم کو فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا اس لئے عملیہ کی قید کے ساتھ احتراز کیا جاتا ہے اسی طرح علم بالبد یہیات کو بھی اصطلاحا فقہ نہیں کہا جاتا اس لئے لا تعلیم کو نہا من الدین بالضرورۃ کے ساتھ ان سے احتراز کیا جاتا ہے لا بمعنی انه لو حد يحتون ام یعنی اس قید کا یہ مفہوم نہیں ہے کہ اگر اس قید کے ساتھ احترانہ نہ کیا جائے تو یہ لازم آتا ہے کہ جو صرف وجوب صلوۃ و صوم کا عالم ہو تو وہ فقیہ ہو گا جیسے کہ مصنف نے سمجھ رکھا ہے اور اس قید پر اعتراض کر دیا ہے کہ یہ لازم نہیں آتا کیونکہ فقیہ من لہ الفقہ کو کہتے ہیں اور فقہ بعض احکام کے علم کو نہیں کہتے وان قل حتیٰ کہ ایک یا دو مسئلے کے عالم کو فقیہ کہا جائے
His statement: 'and nothing is added to it'—the terminology among the Shafi'is means that in the Shafi'i terminology, knowledge of rulings will be called fiqh only when that knowledge is acquired through reflection (nazar) and inference (istidlal), to the extent that knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting etc., which are well-known in the religion and obvious, known to both the religious and irreligious—their knowledge, according to the Shafi'is, is not counted as terminological fiqh. For this reason, these scholars mention the condition of acquisition (iktisab) or inference (istidlal) in the definition of fiqh. Imam Razi in al-Mahsul added the condition 'those that are not known necessarily from religion' and said that this is to avoid including knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting, because that knowledge is not called fiqh. Meaning it does not enter into the meaning of fiqh terminologically, just as he clarified in the condition of 'amaliyya: that knowledge of the evidentiality of consensus, analogy, and solitary report (khabar wahid) is avoided, even though these are shar'i rulings, but knowledge of those rulings is not called fiqh. For this reason, avoidance is made with the condition of 'amaliyya. Similarly, knowledge of apodictic matters (badihiyyat) is also not called fiqh terminologically; therefore, avoidance is made with 'those that are not known necessarily from religion.' Not in the sense that if avoidance were not made with this condition, it would necessitate that one who knows only the obligation of prayer and fasting would be a faqih, as the author understood and objected to this condition. That is not necessary because a faqih is called one who possesses fiqh, and fiqh is not called knowledge of some rulings, even if few, such that a person knowing one or two issues would be called a faqih.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage expands on the Sharh's treatment of the Shafi'i definition of fiqh. The Shafi'is add a condition that fiqh must be acquired through nazar and istidlal (reflection and inference), i.e., through ijtihad. This excludes knowledge of obvious matters known necessarily from the religion (like the obligation of prayer and fasting), which even non-scholars know. Imam Razi in al-Mahsul used the condition 'that are not known necessarily from religion' to exclude these. Similarly, he used the condition 'amaliyya to exclude knowledge of usul principles (like the proof of ijma, qiyas, khabar wahid) because although these are shar'i rulings, they are not practical. The author of the matn (probably Sadr al-Shariah) objected that this condition would wrongly imply that someone knowing only obvious obligations is not a faqih. But the commentator clarifies that the condition is not necessary to avoid that implication; even without it, one who knows only a few rulings is not called a faqih because fiqh implies comprehensive knowledge. Thus the Shafi'i condition of iktisab is primarily for excluding knowledge that is not acquired through ijtihad, i.e., obvious and necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
اکتساب (iktisāb): Acquisition of knowledge through personal effort, reflection, and inference (nazar wa istidlal).
استدلال (istidlāl): Inference or deduction from evidence.
بدیہیات (badīhiyyāt): Self-evident truths known necessarily without reflection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i definition of fiqh, which of the following is NOT considered fiqh?
A) Knowledge of the obligation of prayer derived from ijtihad.
B) Knowledge of the obligation of prayer known necessarily by all Muslims.
C) Knowledge of the permissibility of analogy deduced from evidence.
D) Knowledge of the prohibition of riba derived from Quran and Hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i condition of iktisab ensures that the term 'fiqh' is reserved for knowledge acquired through juristic effort, excluding self-evident religious knowledge.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Hanafi Critique of Tahayyu' Qarib: Fiqh as Actual Knowledge
Dispute over definition of fiqh – capacity vs. actual knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects defining fiqh as mere capacity (tahayyu') and apply the critique to Ibn al-Hajib's definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is actual knowledge of rulings, not just the capacity to derive them – capacity alone is insufficient.

In this passage, the author engages in a critical debate with Ibn al-Hajib over the definition of fiqh. Ibn al-Hajib defined fiqh as 'al-tahayyu' al-qarib' – the near preparedness to derive rulings. The Hanafi author rejects this, arguing that such preparedness is vague and that a true faqih must possess actual knowledge. We will trace the arguments and see how the Hanafi school defends its position using logical precision and textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing definitions
State what Ibn al-Hajib defines as fiqh and what the Hanafi author proposes as the correct definition.
Ibn al-Hajib: fiqh is knowledge of the sum of rulings meaning capacity (tahayyu'). Hanafi: fiqh is actual knowledge of practical legal rulings from detailed evidences.
2
Analyze the Hanafi objection to tahayyu'
Explain why the author rejects tahayyu' ba'id (remote preparedness) and why tahayyu' qarib is considered undefined.
Tahayyu' ba'id is present in every layperson; tahayyu' qarib lacks a precise standard (majhul al-miqdar).
3
Examine Ibn al-Hajib's refinement
Describe how Ibn al-Hajib interprets tahayyu' qarib as possessing all sources and conditions of ijtihad.
He specifies Arabic grammar, Quran, Hadith, principles, alertness, and knowledge of Shariah secrets.
4
Evaluate the author's four objections
List the four points raised against Ibn al-Hajib's interpretation: (1) some mujtahids never know certain rulings, (2) ijtihad can err, (3) rulings may have no scope for ijtihad, (4) capacity does not guarantee knowledge.
The passage focuses on the first two; the third and fourth are addressed later.
5
Assess the commentator's defense and the Hanafi counter-critique
Explain the commentator's responses (conflict of evidences, preventer, opposing imagination, truth resembling falsehood) and why the Hanafi author finds them weak.
The counter-critique shows that these excuses cannot consistently maintain the capacity definition.

حاصل ہونے کے اس وجہ سے ہو کہ ادلہ میں تعارض ہے یا کوئی مانع عن المعرفہ موجود ہو گیا ہے یا حکم معلوم کرتے وقت و ہم معارض للعقل ہو گیا ہے یا حق مشاکل و مشابه للباطل ہو گیا ہے لہذا معرفت حکم نہ ہو سکی تو ان وجوہ کی بنا پر عدم المعرفة تهور بالمعنی المذکور کے منافی نہیں ہے ۔ شارح نے مصنف " پر رد کرتے ہوئے جو وجوہ پیش کئے ہیں ان میں نظر ہے کیونکہ تہیور قریب کے اندر شرائط اور اسباب و مآخذ کا جامع ہونا جميع الشرائط ہوگا اب جس شخص کے لئے مدت حیات زوال مانع نہیں ہو سکا تو اس کو منصف بالتهور القريب قرار نہیں دیا جا سکتا کیونکہ وہ مستجمع لجميع الشرائط نہ ہوا پھر تو لازم آتا ہے کہ ایسے شخص کو فقیہ نہ کہا جائے حالانکہ وہ بالاتفاق فقیہ کہا جاتا ہے کمالم یدرا بو حنفیہ مدة عمرہ حکم الدھر فی لا کلمہ دھرا اور شارح کا عذر بمعارضة الوهم العقل والا بھی درست نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ جو صاحب ملکہ را سخہ ہے اور تمام شرائط اجتہاد کا جامع ہے وہ علی الدوام والاستمرار معارضہ و ہم میں ایسا مبتلا نہیں رہ سکتا کہ اس کا عقل ہمیشہ مغلوب ہو جائے اور وہم غالب رہے اور ایسا کثیرا لو ہم انسان اجتہاد کر ہی نہیں سکتا۔ اور اسی طرح مشاكلة الحق بالباطل کا صدر بھی درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ اجتماع ماخذ و اسباب شرائط کے تحت یہ ذکر کیا گیا ہے کہ جمیع علوم ضروریہ میں مہارت کے ساتھ تقط خاطر اور معرفة باسرار احكام الشریعت اسے حاصل ہو تو پھر ایسے شخص کے لئے یہ مشاکلہ ہمیشہ نہیں رہ سکتا جس کی وجہ سے مدة العمر تک حکم معلوم نہ ہو سکے یا مبتلا فی الخطار ہے۔ ولا نسلمان شيما من الاحكامراة یہ مصنف کے قول ربما يكون ممالیس للاجتہاد فیہ مساغ پر رد کر رہا ہے خلاصہ رد کا یہ ہے کہ ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ وہ احکام جن میں نص اور اجماع وارد نہیں ان میں سے ایسے احکام بھی ہوتے ہیں کہ ان کے اندر اجتہاد کی مساغ اور گنجائش نہ ہو ہماری اس بات پر حدیث معاذ رضی اللہ عنہ دال ہے جب اس نے نص نہ ہونے کی صورت میں کہا تھا اعتمد علی راتی
So it became clear that by 'each individual' [akl wahid] is meant those rulings that occur in reality, which are presented to the mujtahid and he says 'I do not know' – thus the meaning became clear. In this case, the difference between the collective whole [kulli ijmali] and the individual whole [kulli fardi] is established, so the comparison between them is correct. The reality is that the author's intention from these two branches was exactly what the commentator presented with 'and the apparent' – the commentator unnecessarily lengthened, then finally returned to this, which was actually the author's intention; so this extension is without benefit. However, this much benefit certainly occurred: some rulings of the collective whole and individual whole became clear. And when Ibn al-Hajib responded to this objection, he said: In the definition of fiqh, 'knowledge of the sum of rulings' is intended, and 'knowledge of the sum of rulings' means 'al-tahayyu' li dhalik' i.e., the ability and preparedness to know the sum of rulings exists. The author rejected it, saying: 'al-tahayyu' al-ba'id' is also possessed by a non-faqih, and 'al-tahayyu' al-qarib' is not firmly defined – meaning it has no specific quantity that defines it, so it is unknown in measure. And when Ibn al-Hajib interpreted 'al-tahayyu'', he interpreted 'al-tahayyu' al-qarib' as 'be such a person' i.e., a person's state and rank such that he can know the ruling of every incident through ijtihad, due to his gathering the sources, i.e., all the sources, causes, and conditions by which ability and power to acquire rulings is achieved, and turning to them is sufficient for knowledge of rulings – he possesses all of them. By sources and causes is meant: that he obtains from the materials of ijtihad and its causes, from Arabic [grammar], morphological and grammatical rules, knowledge of the Quran and Hadith and their principles, along with alertness of mind and knowledge of the secrets of the rulings of Shariah. The author rejected this interpretation with four aspects. It is possible to respond, meaning that a response can be given to the four aspects with which he rejected it. So the commentator, detailing these responses, said: 'We do not accept' – these are the first two aspects of the author: one, that some rulings were unknown to the mujtahid scholars throughout their lives; the second, that error also occurs in ijtihad – so al-tahayyu' al-qarib did not provide the benefit of knowledge of rulings. The commentator rejected them, saying: We do not accept that this non-availability of knowledge of some rulings and error in ijtihad is contrary to al-tahayyu' in the mentioned sense. We do not accept it, because it is possible that this non-availability and error occur due to three reasons: [1] conflict of evidences, [2] existence of a preventer from knowledge, [3] at the time of knowing the ruling, imagination opposing reason, [4] truth being similar to falsehood. Therefore, knowledge of the ruling was not achieved. Thus, based on these reasons, non-knowledge is not contrary to al-tahayyu' in the mentioned sense. There is consideration in the aspects that the commentator presented while rejecting the author, because al-tahayyu' al-qarib requires gathering conditions and causes and sources. So the commentator's presentation of conflict of evidences as a proof is not correct, because conflict of evidences does not necessitate ignorance of the ruling; at the time of conflict, if one evidence is given priority, the ruling is established according to it; if priority is not possible, then – the commentator said in the discussion of conflict – it makes the two evidences as nonexistent; so another evidence is sought or ruling by analogy is made, or at least in this case suspension is also a ruling. And presenting the excuse of existence of a preventer is also not correct, because the preventer's preventiveness means its opposite is a condition. So when al-tahayyu' al-qarib requires gathering conditions, then removal of preventers is included in the existence of all conditions. Now, a person for whom the removal of the preventer does not occur during his lifetime cannot be considered as possessing al-tahayyu' al-qarib, because he did not gather all conditions. Then it would follow that such a person is not called a faqih, while he is unanimously called a faqih, just as Abu Hanifa did not know during his lifetime the ruling of the nail in a certain issue. And the commentator's excuse of imagination opposing reason is also not correct, because the one who possesses a firmly rooted faculty [malakah rasikhah] and gathers all conditions of ijtihad cannot be constantly afflicted with opposing imagination such that his reason is always overwhelmed and imagination prevails; and such a person cannot frequently perform ijtihad. Similarly, the excuse of similarity of truth with falsehood is also not correct, because it is mentioned under the gathering of sources and causes that he obtains with proficiency in all necessary sciences, alertness of mind, and knowledge of the secrets of the rulings of Shariah. Then for such a person, this similarity cannot persist such that rulings remain unknown for his entire life, or he is constantly in error. And 'we do not accept any of the rulings' – this is a response to the author's statement 'perhaps there are rulings that have no room for ijtihad'. The summary of the response is that we do not accept that among rulings that have no explicit text or consensus, there are some rulings that have no scope for ijtihad. The hadith of Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him) indicates this, when he said, in the absence of an explicit text, 'I rely on my opinion.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute concerning the correct definition of fiqh (jurisprudence). The discussion arises from an earlier objection regarding the terms 'kulli ijmali' (collective whole) and 'kulli fardi' (individual whole) in the definition of fiqh. The author (al-Muṣannif, likely Sadr al-Shariah) had defined fiqh as knowledge of rulings. Ibn al-Hajib objected, saying that 'knowledge of the sum of rulings' means 'al-tahayyu' li dhalik' – i.e., the capacity or preparedness to know them. The author rejected this, arguing that 'al-tahayyu' al-ba'id' is too vague (even a non-faqih has it), and 'al-tahayyu' al-qarib' lacks a precise measure. Ibn al-Hajib then refined his definition: al-tahayyu' al-qarib means being such that one can derive rulings from evidence, possessing all necessary tools like Arabic grammar, Quran, Hadith, principles, mental alertness, and knowledge of the secrets of Shariah. The author counters with four objections: (1) some mujtahids never know certain rulings, (2) ijtihad can err, so this capacity does not guarantee knowledge. The commentator (al-Sharif al-Jurjani? Actually here the commentator is responding) defends Ibn al-Hajib by saying these objections are not valid because the failure to know may be due to external factors like conflicting evidences, a precluding factor, opposing imagination, or truth resembling falsehood. The text then critiques the commentator's defense: conflict of evidences does not necessitate ignorance because one can prefer or seek other evidence; a precluding factor means the person lacks the capacity; opposing imagination cannot persist in a true mujtahid; truth's similarity is overcome by deep knowledge. The final part addresses another objection: the author claimed there are rulings with no room for ijtihad; this is rejected using the hadith of Mu'adh, where he said he would rely on his own reasoning in the absence of text. This is a strong Hanafi proof for the legitimacy of qiyas and ijtihad in all non-textual matters. The passage thus argues that fiqh is actual knowledge, not mere capacity, and that capacity must be defined precisely. The Hanafi position is that fiqh is the knowledge of practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidences, not just preparedness.

Key Terms
التہیؤ القریب (al-tahayyu' al-qarib): The near preparedness: being such that one can derive rulings from evidence due to possessing all necessary tools of ijtihad.
التہیؤ البعید (al-tahayyu' al-ba'id): The remote preparedness: simply having the general ability to learn fiqh, possessed even by a non-faqih.
کل مجموعی (kulli ijmali): The collective whole: the sum of rulings considered not one by one but as a whole body of law.
مساغ الاجتہاد (masagh al-ijtihad): Scope for ijtihad: rulings that allow for independent reasoning due to absence of definitive text or consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main Hanafi objection to defining fiqh as 'al-tahayyu' al-qarib'?
A) It is too specific.
B) It is vague and lacks a precise measure.
C) It is too broad.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines fiqh as actual knowledge of practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidences, not mere capacity.

Dispute on the Meaning of 'Ilm in Fiqh Definition
Critique and defense of using 'ilm to mean malakah in fiqh definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions in the dispute over whether 'ilm in the definition of fiqh denotes perception (idrak) or faculty (malakah), apply the Hanafi usul principle that qualified 'ilm indicates perception, and evaluate the strength of the sharh's objection using the hadith of Mu'adh.

Core Idea
Qualified 'ilm (e.g., 'ilm bi'l-ahkam) means perception, not faculty.

This passage engages with a dispute between the author of al-Tawdih and the commentator of al-Talwih regarding the word 'ilm in the standard Hanafi definition of fiqh: 'knowledge of the practical rulings from their detailed evidences'. At stake is whether 'ilm refers to the actual perception of rulings or the faculty (malakah) that enables one to derive rulings. The author argues for perception because 'ilm is qualified by its object. The commentator counters that 'ilm can denote faculty, as in common usage and the analogy 'knowledge is like life'. The present writer refutes the commentator, insisting that qualified 'ilm loses its ability to denote the faculty. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to interpret usul definitions critically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The author (musannif al-Tawdih) holds that 'ilm in the definition means actual perception (idrak) of rulings. The commentator (sharh al-Talwih) argues it can mean the faculty (malakah) of istinbat.
The definition is: 'al-ilm bi'l-ahkam al-shar'iyyah min adillatiha al-tafṣiliyyah'.
2
Examine the sharh's evidence
The sharh cites (1) common usage: calling the ability to know as 'knowledge', and (2) the analogy 'al-ilm ka al-hayah' (knowledge is like life), where life is a faculty for perception.
The analogy is meant to show that 'ilm can be a cause or instrument (i.e., a faculty).
3
Analyze the response to the sharh
The present writer argues that when 'ilm is qualified by its object (here: bi'l-ahkam), it exclusively denotes perception. The analogy uses absolute 'ilm, so it does not apply. Some add that the analogy refers to principles and rules, not a faculty.
This is a key usul principle: qualified terms lose absolute generality.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If 'ilm means faculty, then a person who knows rulings by rote without the ability to derive them is not a faqih. If it means perception, such a person could be called a faqih if they perceive the rulings from evidence.
This affects the definition of faqih and the scope of ijtihad.

Source Text

تو نبی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے یہ تو نہیں فرمایا تھا کہ فان لم یکن محلاً للاجتہاد تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ نص اور اجماع نہ ہونے کی صورت میں ہر حکم محل اجتہاد ہو سکتا ہے ۔ قیل فی جوا بہ حدیث معاذ رضی اللہ جہتی اور اپ ہے یعنی جیسے حیوۃ ذریعہ اور سبب ادراک ہے اسی طرح علم بھی جیتے اور ذریعہ اور اک ہے تو اس تشبیہ میں علم سے مراد ملکہ ہے نہ کہ نفس اور اک کیونکہ جہتہ اور اک تو ملکہ ہی ہو سکتا ہے نہ نفس اور اک شارح کے اس رد کا جواب یہ دیا جاتا ہے کہ اطلاق علم علی الملکہ اس وقت شائع ذائع ہے جب لفظ علم مطلق بولا جائے اور اس کا متعلق یعنی معلوم نہ مذ کو ہو نہ مقدر جب لفظ علم کے ساتھ متعلق مذکور ہو یا مقدر تو اس وقت صرف معنی ادراک علی التعيين مراد ہوتا ہے اور ملکہ مراد نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں تعریف فقہ میں علم بالاحکام کہا گیا ہے اور علم کا متعلق احکام مذکور ہے اس لئے یہاں صرف ادراک مراد ہو سکتا ہے ملکہ مراد بعض حضرات نے یہ بھی کہا ہے کہ العلم کا لحیوۃ میں علم سے مراد ملکہ نہیں ہے بلکہ اصول و قواعد مراد ہیں کیونکہ یہ بھی ذریعہ اور جبہتہ اور اک ہیں جیسے حیوۃ جہتہ ادراک ہے لہذا شارح کا اس سے استدلال کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے علاوہ ازیں جو تعریف فقہ میں من ادلتہا التفصیلیہ کی قید لگائی گئی یہ بھی متعین کرتی ہے یہاں علم سے مراد اور اک ہے ملکہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ تصریح کی گئی ہے کہ من ادلتہا کا تعلق علم سے ہے تو جو علم بالشی دلیل سے حاصل ہوتا ہے وہ ادراک بالشتی ہے نہ کہ ملکہ بالشتی لہذا مصنف کی رائے بالکل صحیح ہے کہ اس مقام پر لا دلالہ للفظ العلم على التهور المخصوص اصلا اور بشارح مقام لا نے جتنا اعتراضات کہتے ہیں وہ ساقط ہیں ۔
Thus the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not say 'if it is not a place for ijtihad', so it is understood that in the absence of text and consensus, every ruling can be a place for ijtihad. It was said in response: the hadith of Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him) does not indicate that, because it is possible that his not saying 'if there is no room for ijtihad' indicates the apparent ruling of referring back to him concerning what has no room for ijtihad. And we do not accept that there is no indication of the word 'ilm — the author objected to taking 'ilm to mean a specific [thing] — that there is no indication of the word at all, that the word 'ilm does not indicate the specific [thing]. The commentator is refuting this, saying: we do not accept that there is no indication, meaning we do not accept that the word 'ilm does not indicate the specific [thing], because the intended meaning is the faculty (malakah) by which one gains the power to perceive the particulars of rulings, and the application of 'ilm to this faculty is widespread in custom, just as in definitions of knowledge one says 'knowledge of such and such', and the verifiers hold that this faculty is intended, just as 'craft' is said. And 'ilm does not mean the essence of perception. Similarly, what is said 'al-ilm ka al-hayah' (knowledge is like life), in that the point of comparison is [؟] and [؟]; that is, just as life is a means and cause of perception, similarly knowledge is a means and cause of perception. So in this analogy, 'ilm means the faculty, not the essence of perception, because the means and cause can only be the faculty, not the essence of perception. The response to this refutation of the commentator is: the application of 'ilm to the faculty is widespread only when the word 'ilm is used absolutely and its object (known thing) is neither mentioned nor implied. When the object is mentioned or implied, then only the meaning of perception specifically is intended, and the faculty is not intended. Here, in the definition of fiqh, it is said 'knowledge of the rulings', and the object (rulings) is mentioned, so here only perception can be intended, not the faculty. In the analogy of 'al-ilm ka al-hayah', since 'ilm is mentioned absolutely, the faculty can be intended there. In the definition of fiqh, the word 'ilm is not absolute but qualified by the object, so taking it to mean the faculty is not correct. And some have also said that in 'al-ilm ka al-hayah', 'ilm does not mean the faculty but rather the principles and rules, because these are also a means and cause of perception, like life is a cause of perception. Therefore, the commentator's argument from this is not correct. Moreover, the condition 'from its detailed evidences' in the definition of fiqh also determines that here 'ilm means perception, not the faculty, because it is specified that 'from its evidences' relates to 'ilm, so the knowledge obtained from evidence is perception of the thing, not the faculty of the thing. Hence the author's opinion is absolutely correct that in this place there is no indication of the word 'ilm for the specific [meaning], and the objections raised by the commentator are invalid.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the meaning of 'ilm in the definition of fiqh. The author (musannif) of al-Tawdih objected that the word 'ilm cannot specifically mean the faculty (malakah) of istinbat. The commentator (sharh) argued that it can, by citing common usage and the analogy 'al-ilm ka al-hayah'. The present commentator (the writer of this text) refutes the sharh, arguing that when 'ilm is qualified (e.g., 'ilm bi'l-ahkam), it refers to actual perception (idrak), not the faculty. He also points out that the analogy with life does not prove the faculty, as some scholars interpret it as referring to principles and rules. The writer concludes that the author's position is correct: there is no indication in the word 'ilm for the specific meaning of malakah.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm): In this passage, 'ilm is debated: the musannif says it means perception (idrak), the sharh says it can mean faculty (malakah).
ملکہ (malakah): A faculty or ability to derive rulings from evidences.
اجتہاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning in the absence of clear text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When 'ilm is qualified by its object, it denotes perception, not the faculty of deriving rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Specificity of Fiqh: Personal vs Generic (Ta'yin Shakhsi vs Naw'i)
First objection: definition lacks specificity; answer: genre-specificity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'yin shakhsi and ta'yin naw'i and apply the Hanafi view that fiqh is a genre-specific science admitting increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Fiqh is specific in type, not quantity: its subject defines it, not its fixed number of rulings.

The commentator defends the author's definition of fiqh against the charge that it makes fiqh a universal, changing concept. He clarifies that 'specific science' means genre-specific (ta'yin naw'i), not personally specific (ta'yin shakhsi). This distinction preserves the dynamic nature of fiqh while maintaining its identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first objection: the author's definition makes fiqh a universal concept that changes with time, whereas technical terms are specific and fixed.
Understand the charge: the definition seems to lack specificity.
2
Distinguish the two types of specificity
The commentator introduces ta'yin shakhsi (personal specificity) and ta'yin naw'i (generic specificity). Determine which one applies to fiqh.
Ta'yin shakhsi = fixed number of rulings; ta'yin naw'i = defined by subject and essential attributes.
3
Apply the distinction
Explain why ta'yin shakhsi is not intended: all compiled sciences are universal and admit increase. Fiqh is a 'alam jinsi (generic proper noun), not a 'alam shakhsi.
The names of sciences are like 'Fiqh' or 'Nahw'—they refer to a type, not a fixed set.
4
Provide the Hanafi evidence
The Companions were jurists despite their fiqh increasing with revelation and decreasing with abrogation. This shows that increase and decrease do not negate specificity.
Knowledge of naskh preserves the jurist's competence.
5
Conclude
Specificity is about the subject (acts of the mukallaf ) and essential attributes, not about a fixed quantity. Thus the definition stands.
The objector's argument conflates quantitative fixity with qualitative specificity.

جاتی تھی بہر حال زيادة و نقص تعیین نوعی کے منافی نہیں ہے علاوہ ازیں نسخ کی صورت میں علم بالناسخ والمنسوخ مجتہد فقیہ کے لئے ضروری ہے تو اس صورت میں نقص في العلم واقع نہیں ہوگا اور زیادۃ فی العلوم تو ہوتی رہتی ہے جس کا انکار نہیں کیا جا سکتا۔ الثاني إن التعريف الا يه دوسرا اعتراض ہے حاصل اس کا يه ہے کہ يه تعریف فقہ صحابہ پر صادق نہیں آتی کیونکہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی زندگی میں اجماع کا وجود ہی نہیں تھا حالانکہ مصنف نے علم بالاحكام التي انعقد علیہ الاجماع کو ضروری قرار دیا ہے لہزار سول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے زمانہ میں جو صحابہ فقیہ تھے ان کی فقہ پر يه تعریف صادق نہیں آئے گی ۔ کانہ اراد گویا کہ مصنف کی مراد يه ہوگی کہ فقہ صرف علم بما ظہر بہ نزول الوحی کا نام ہے اگر اجماع نہ ہو اور اگر جماع ہو تو پھر علم بما ظہر بہ نزول الوحی اور بما انعقد علیہ الاجماع کا نام فقہ ہو گا اور اس قسم کی تشکیکات تعریفات میں بعید ہیں۔ اس کا جواب دیا گیا ہے کہ مصنف کی مراد يهی مفہوم ہے کما صرح به في التوست ولا بعد في التعريف بمثل ہذا کیونکہ يه امر مشہور ہے کہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے زمانہ میں صحابہ کے اندر فقہ اور اجتہاد موجود تھا حالانکہ اس وقت اجماع کا وجود نہیں تھا تو اس سے واضح ہو جاتا ہے مراد يهی ہے کہ علم بما انعقد علیہ الاجتماع اس وقت ضروری ہے جب اجتماع موجود ہو اگر موجود نہ ہو تو اس کا علم شرط نہیں ہے اس شہرت کی وجہ سے اس مراد میں کوئی بعد نہیں ہے علاوہ ازیں مصنف کا قول والتي انعقد علیہ الاجماع سے مراد ہے والتي انعقد علیہ الاجماع عند تحققہ تو اب مفہوم تعریف يه ہوگا کہ فقہ علم ہے بماظہر يه نزول الوحی کا در آنحالیکہ وہ مقرون ہو علم بالاحکام التي انعقد علیہ الاجماع
Here begin investigations: the commentator, as per his custom, starts a series of objections against the author. First objection: the purpose was to define fiqh as it is a technical term among the people, but the author's definition cannot be the technical fiqh because it is, according to them, a name for a specific science, like other sciences are specific sciences. According to what the author mentioned, fiqh would be a name for a universal concept that changes with times and eras and increases day by day until the end of the prophetic era, because in the time of the Prophet, revelation came down gradually, so according to that the knowledge of fiqh would increase day by day; then after the Prophet, it would continue to increase due to the occurrence of ijma'. Also it would decrease according to abrogating verses, because in the time of the Prophet abrogation occurred, so in one respect it decreases; and after the Prophet, if ijma' goes against a solitary report, that report would be abrogated, so with its abrogation a decrease occurs. In any case, according to the author's definition, fiqh is not a specific and determined science, whereas it is a specific and determined science. Answer to the first objection: what does the commentator mean by 'a name for a specific science'? If he means specific in a personal sense (ta'yin shakhsi) that is limited to a fixed number of rulings not subject to increase, this meaning is not correct because all compiled sciences are universal sciences that are subject to increase from time to time with the succession of thoughts. That is why the names of sciences are called generic proper nouns (a'lam jinsiyya), not personal proper nouns. If by 'specific' he means specific in a generic sense (ta'yin naw'i), i.e., it discusses the essential attributes of one subject by which it is distinguished from other sciences, then this kind of specificity is not contradictory to change over times and eras; rather, it can admit increase and decrease. After all, the Companions who are agreed upon as jurists: their knowledge used to increase gradually according to the revelation of rulings, and when a ruling was abrogated, a decrease occurred. So increase and decrease do not contravene generic specificity. Moreover, in the case of abrogation, knowledge of the abrogating and abrogated is necessary for the mujtahid jurist, so in that case there is no actual decrease in knowledge, and increase in sciences continues to happen, which cannot be denied. Second objection: this definition does not apply to the fiqh of the Companions because during the life of the Prophet there was no ijma', yet the author made knowledge of rulings upon which ijma' occurred a necessary part of fiqh. Therefore, the definition does not apply to the Companions who were jurists in the Prophet's time. [It is as if] the author intended that fiqh is only knowledge of what is revealed if there is no ijma'; but if there is ijma', then fiqh becomes knowledge of what is revealed and knowledge of what is agreed upon. Such doubts are far-fetched in definitions. The answer is that the author's intention is indeed this meaning, as he stated in al-Tawdih, and there is no oddity in such a definition, because it is well-known that during the Prophet's time the Companions had fiqh and ijtihad even though ijma' did not exist then. From this it becomes clear that the intention is that knowledge of rulings upon which ijma' is concluded is necessary when ijma' exists; if it does not exist, then such knowledge is not a condition. Due to this well-known fact, there is no remoteness in this intention. Besides, the author's statement 'and which are agreed upon' means 'and which are agreed upon when it occurs'. So now the meaning of the definition would be: fiqh is knowledge of what appears with revelation, while it is conjoined with knowledge of rulings upon which ijma' is concluded.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute presenting two objections to the definition of fiqh given by the author (Sadr al-Shariah) and their refutation by the commentator. The first objection argues that the author's definition makes fiqh a universal concept that changes with time, whereas technically fiqh is a specific and fixed science. The response clarifies that 'specific' does not mean person-specific (limited to a fixed number) but genre-specific (distinct subject and essential attributes). All sciences, including fiqh, can increase and decrease without losing their specificity; the Companions' fiqh grew with revelation and decreased with abrogation. The second objection claims the definition fails for the Prophet's time because ijma' did not exist. The answer is that the definition implicitly includes ijma' only when it occurs, as known from the well-established fact that the Companions had fiqh before ijma'. The commentator thus defends the author's definition as logically sound and consistent with the historical development of fiqh.

Key Terms
تعیین شخصی (ta'yin shakhsi): Individual/personal specification where a science is limited to a fixed number of rulings.
تعیین نوعی (ta'yin naw'i): Generic specification where a science is defined by its subject and essential attributes, allowing variation in quantity.
علم بما ظهر به نزول الوحی (‘ilm bimā ẓahara bihi nuzūl al-waḥy): Knowledge of rulings that are established through direct revelation to the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's answer to the first objection, what type of specificity (ta'yin) does the definition of fiqh have?
A) Ta'yin shakhsi (personal specificity)
B) Ta'yin naw'i (generic specificity)
C) No specificity
D) Ta'yin kulli (universal specificity)

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is defined by its subject and essential attributes, allowing for growth and change across eras without losing its specificity.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Role of Ijma' in the Definition of Fiqh
Second objection: definition fails for era without ijma'; answer: conditional inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the definition of fiqh accommodates the absence of ijma' during the Prophet's time and evaluate the objection that it excludes the Companions' fiqh.

Core Idea
The definition includes ijma' only when it exists; it is not a constant requirement for fiqh.

A second objection argues that the author's definition requiring knowledge of ijma' cannot apply to the Companions during the Prophet's lifetime, when ijma' did not exist. The commentator answers that the phrase 'which are agreed upon' assumes ijma' when it occurs; if no ijma', it is not required. This is supported by the well-known fact that the Companions had fiqh and ijtihad without ijma'.

Guided Path
1
State the second objection
The objector argues that the definition requires knowledge of rulings upon which ijma' is concluded, but during the Prophet's time there was no ijma', so the definition fails to include the Companions' fiqh.
Understand the challenge: the definition seems anachronistic for the earliest period of Islam.
2
Identify the implicit assumption
The objector assumes that the definition includes knowledge of ijma' as an unconditional requirement at all times.
The objection only holds if the definition demands ijma' knowledge always.
3
Present the commentator's resolution
The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'which are agreed upon' means 'which are agreed upon when it occurs'. The definition is conditional: if ijma' exists, knowledge of it is part of fiqh; if not, it is not required.
This conditional interpretation resolves the anachronism.
4
Support with historical evidence
It is well-known that the Companions were jurists and mujtahids during the Prophet's time without ijma'. This shows that the definition does not require ijma' knowledge where it does not exist.
The commentator uses the historical fact as proof that the definition is correctly understood.
5
Conclude
The definition is consistent: it includes ijma' knowledge only when ijma' is present. There is no oddity (bu'd) in this conditional formulation.
The author's definition stands refined.
