Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi View on Marriage as Ownership and Cause-Effect Reasoning
Usul al-fiqh dispute on marriage nature and cause-effect metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that marriage establishes ownership, identify the proofs from dower and divorce, and apply the sabab/musabbab principle to evaluate the validity of marriage contracts using sale or gift words.

Core Idea
Marriage is ownership, not mere benefits; cause-effect allows sale words to create marriage.

In this dense passage, the Hanafi school defends its view that marriage is fundamentally about the husband acquiring ownership (milk) over his wife, contrary to the Shafi'i emphasis on mutual benefits. This disagreement has practical consequences: Hanafis permit marriage using the words of sale or gift, while Shafi'is require the specific terms 'nikah' or 'tazwij'. The reasoning hinges on a sophisticated use of cause-effect (sabab/musabbab) relationships and the notion of technical terms (ʿalam). This lesson unpacks the core arguments, objections, and the limits of the metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage's opening argument. What do the Hanafis claim is the primary purpose of marriage? What do the Shafi'is claim?
Hanafi: husband's ownership (milk). Shafi'i: mutual benefits (masalih).
2
Extract the Hanafi proof from dower and divorce
Find the sentence that says dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his control. How does that prove ownership?
These rights are exclusive to an owner; thus marriage establishes ownership.
3
Understand technical terms (ʿalam)
The words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' are not literally about ownership in language, yet they are valid. Why? Because they have become technical terms (ʿalam) for the marriage contract in Shariah.
Technical terms need not follow literal meanings.
4
Analyze the cause-effect (sabab/musabbab) application
The passage says marriage is valid with the word 'hibah' (gift) or 'bay' (sale). The reasoning: sale is a cause for ownership of substance (milk al-raqaba), which is a cause for ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'a) – i.e., marriage. So using the name of the cause (sale) intending the effect (marriage) is valid.
This is a form of qiyas jali: analogy based on causal link.
5
Examine the reverse metaphor objection and its resolution
The opponent argues: if cause-effect works one way, why not the reverse? Why can't we use 'nikah' intending 'sale'? The Hanafi answer: reverse metaphor is only valid when the cause is legislated specifically for that effect. In sale, ownership is the primary objective, so 'sale' can be used for 'ownership'. But ownership of a slave is not legislated for the purpose of marriage, so 'nikah' cannot be used for 'sale'.
The conditional emancipation example illustrates this principle.

The verse's meaning is that the validity of marriage with the wording of perplexity without obligation of dower is exclusive to you. And for other than the Prophet, marriage will be contracted with the word 'hibah' but dower becomes obligatory. And also it is possible that this is the second answer for 'khāliṣatan laka'. The summary is that this verse can also mean this. And Allah knows best what He intends by that. The summary is that 'khāliṣatan laka' is connected to 'innā ahlalnā laka azwājak' and in terms of composition it is a circumstantial accusative for 'azwājak'. The meaning would be: 'We have made lawful for you your wives, a state that is exclusive to you' i.e., the wives of the Prophet are exclusive to him and it is not permissible for anyone else to marry them. As Allah said about them: 'and his wives are their mothers' whether the Prophet divorces them during his lifetime, God forbid, or after death, these pure wives cannot marry anyone. So these pure wives are exclusively for him during his life and after his death. Because the word (i.e., exclusivity and specificity) is not in the wording. Because metaphor is specific to the Prophet? This is a linguistic and verbal issue; it cannot be specific to anyone. And also those generic matters: This is the answer to Imam Shafi'i's second proof that the legislation of marriage is for benefits. The answer is that the legislation and establishment of marriage is not for these benefits, rather these are branches and fruits of marriage. The foundation and establishment of marriage is that the husband acquires ownership over the wife. That is why a dower becomes obligatory on the husband in exchange for the ownership of marriage, and divorce is also in the husband's control. So this is clear proof that he is the owner and the marriage contract is for establishing ownership. If its establishment were for the sake of these benefits, then all these benefits are shared between spouses, so the dower would not be obligatory on the husband and the right of divorce would not be exclusive to the husband. So when dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his ownership and control, it is known that the establishment of marriage is for the husband's ownership over the wife. And when marriage is valid with two words that in language are not indicative of ownership, i.e., the words 'nikah' and 'tazwij', then it is more fitting that it be valid with words that indicate ownership, like the word 'hibah' etc., so the contract of marriage a fortiori should be valid with them. And it is only valid with them, i.e., marriage is valid with the words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' because they have become technical terms (ʿalam) for this contract. This is the answer to an objection. The objection is that when the words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' in language do not indicate ownership, then marriage should not be valid with them because they indicate nor are they established for ownership. So the answer is that these two words have become like proper nouns for this legal contract, i.e., just as a proper noun is established for a specific named thing, similarly in Shariah these words have become established for this contract, and in proper nouns, observance of the literal meaning is not necessary. And similarly marriage is contracted with the word 'hibah'. This is an appendage to 'and similarly marriage of others according to us'. I.e., just as marriage is contracted with the word 'hibah', similarly it will be contracted with the word 'sale'. For example, a free, sane, adult woman says 'sell yourself to me' and the addressee accepts, then marriage is contracted. Here also the causal relationship (ʿalāqa sababiyya) is at work: sale is established for ownership of the substance (milk al-raqaba) and that is the cause of ownership of usufruct (milk al-mutʿa), so applying the name of the cause (sabab) intending the effect (musabbab) means ownership of usufruct, which is the marriage contract. If it is said: the reverse should also be established, i.e., by causal relationship, applying the name of the effect intending the cause should be valid, so that applying the word 'nikah' intending sale or gift should be valid by way of using the effect for the cause. Since marriage is established for ownership of usufruct which is the effect of ownership of substance, therefore mentioning the effect intending the cause should be valid. We say: it is only like that in a case where the cause is legislated for the sake of the effect, i.e., this cause is legislated so that this effect is established as the primary objective. In short, the primary objective of the legislation of the cause is that very ruling which is the effect. Like sale for ownership: sale is a cause legislated for ownership, and ownership is the effect. Ownership is the primary objective of sale because ownership is the final cause of sale. In such a place, metaphor from both sides can be valid. Unlike ownership of substance: its legislation's primary objective is not ownership of usufruct, nor is ownership of substance established for its acquisition. For example, a slave or a Magian or a polytheist slave girl can come into ownership, but here ownership of usufruct is not attained. So it is known that ownership of substance is not a cause that is legislated for the effect, i.e., ownership of usufruct. In such places, metaphor from both sides cannot apply. The next issue provides more clarification. Consider it; it is this: If someone says: 'in milkta 'abdan' (if you own a slave) or says: 'in ishtarayta 'abdan' (if you buy a slave) then he is free. So he bought the slave in parts, e.g., half today and half the next day. In the case of saying 'in ishtarayta', the slave will be free, but in the first case ('in milkta') he will not be free. Explaining, he said: In the case of 'in milkta 'abdan', he bought half of the slave and sold it, then later bought the other half. In this 'in milkta' scenario, the second half will not be free because the condition of owning the complete slave is not fulfilled since after selling the first half, he is not attributed with owning the slave when buying the second half, so he will not be free. But if he said 'in ishtarayta 'abdan' and bought half and then later bought the second half, the second half will be free because upon buying, he is attributed with the attribute of 'buyer of the slave' because customarily he will be called 'buyer of slaves'. This issue is based on the principle that derived adjectives (e.g., active participle and passive participle and attribute...)
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the nature of the marriage contract. The Hanafi position is that marriage is fundamentally about establishing the husband's ownership (milk) over the wife, not merely about achieving shared benefits (masalih). The proof is that the dower is obligatory on the husband and the right of divorce is exclusively in his hands, both of which are characteristics of ownership. The Shafi'i view that marriage is for benefits is refuted: benefits are secondary results, not the primary purpose. The text then addresses the validity of marriage using different words. The words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' have become technical terms (ʿalam) in Shariah for the marriage contract, so they are valid even if their literal meanings do not indicate ownership. Furthermore, by the principle of 'cause-effect relationship' (sabab wa musabbab), marriage is valid with the words 'hibah' and 'sale' (bay') because these words denote cause (ownership of substance) which leads to the effect (ownership of usufruct in marriage). The opponent argues that if cause-effect works, then the reverse (using the effect word for the cause) should also be valid. The Hanafi response is that this is only valid when the cause is legislated specifically for that effect. In sale, ownership (milk) is the primary objective, so the word 'sale' can be used for 'ownership' metaphorically. However, in the case of ownership of a slave (milk al-raqaba), that ownership is not legislated specifically for the purpose of obtaining ownership of usufruct (milk al-mutʿa) in marriage, because one can own a slave without having marital rights. Therefore, the reverse metaphor does not apply. The passage concludes with a practical example: conditional emancipation. If someone says 'if you own a slave, he is free' versus 'if you buy a slave, he is free', the conditions are interpreted differently. When said 'if you buy', buying in parts still triggers the condition because the person is called a buyer. But 'if you own' requires complete ownership at the moment of fulfillment, so partial ownership after sale does not trigger it. This illustrates the principle of derived adjectives and how the sabab/musabbab relationship affects legal rulings.

Key Terms
ملک رقبہ (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the substance of a thing, e.g., ownership of a slave's body.
ملک متعہ (milk al-mutʿah): Ownership of usufruct or benefit, such as the husband's right to enjoy his wife.
علاقہ سببیہ (ʿalāqa sababiyya): Causal relationship between a cause (sabab) and its effect (musabbab).
علم (ʿalam): A proper noun or technical term that directly indicates a specific meaning without need for literal interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi reasoning in the passage, why is a marriage contract valid when a woman says 'sell yourself to me' and the man accepts?
A) Because the word 'sell' literally means marriage in Arabic.
B) Because the word 'sell' is a technical term (ʿalam) for marriage.
C) Because sale is the cause (sabab) of ownership of substance, and marriage is ownership of usufruct, so using the name of the cause for the effect is valid.
D) Because all contracts are considered marriage if there is mutual consent.

Key Takeaway
Marriage is a contract of ownership, and Hanafis permit using words of sale or gift due to the cause-effect relationship between ownership of substance and ownership of usufruct.

Tasdiq When Intention Differs in Ownership Oath
Ruling on accepting a person's claim of intended meaning diyanatan and qada'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for tasdiq when a person's claimed intention differs from the literal meaning, distinguishing between diyanatan and qada'an, and apply it to ownership oath scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a person's claim of intended meaning is accepted diyanatan always, but qada'an only if it imposes a stricter ruling on himself.

This passage presents a key Hanafi usul rule regarding the acceptance (tasdiq) of a person's claim about his intended meaning in legal statements, specifically oaths related to ownership of a slave. The Hanafi school distinguishes between diyanatan (before Allah, based on truthfulness) and qada'an (in court, based on apparent evidence). The rule is applied to two scenarios involving the words 'in milktu' (if I own) and 'in ishtaraytu' (if I buy), where the person later claims he meant the other term metaphorically. The outcome depends on whether the claimed interpretation makes the ruling stricter or lighter for the speaker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (a) speaker says 'in milktu' but claims he meant 'in ishtaraytu' (metaphor: effect for cause); (b) speaker says 'in ishtaraytu' but claims he meant 'in milktu' (metaphor: cause for effect).
2
Determine the legal consequence of each literal meaning
Under literal meaning, 'in milktu' does not free the second half (stricter for the speaker if he intends to free), while 'in ishtaraytu' does free (lighter for the speaker if he intends to avoid freeing).
Strictness/lightness is from the speaker's perspective regarding the resulting obligation.
3
Apply the tasdiq rule
If the claimed interpretation leads to a stricter outcome for the speaker (scenario a), he is believed both diyanatan and qada'an. If it leads to a lighter outcome (scenario b), he is believed only diyanatan, not qada'an.
The key criterion is whether the speaker stands to benefit (avoid an obligation) by the claim.
4
Understand the rationale
The rule prevents the speaker from escaping a binding oath by claiming a far-fetched interpretation. When he claims a stricter interpretation, he is not suspected of lying because he is imposing a greater burden on himself.
This is a principle of 'al-mar'u musaddaqun fi niyyatihi' (a person is believed in his intention) but with the qada'an exception for self-serving claims.
5
Note the linguistic validity
The metaphor (cause for effect or effect for cause) is linguistically valid, but the court's rejection does not affect its correctness. The legal ruling is based on policy (sadd al-dhara'i), not on language.
The passage explicitly states: 'This does not affect the validity of the isti'arah.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال عنيت بأحدهما الآخر أي إذا قال عنيت بالملك الشراء أو بالشراء الملك صدق ديانةً لا قضاءً حيث يكون في حقه تخفيف. يعني في صورة إن ملکت عبداً فقال عنيت بالملك الشراء بطريق إطلاق اسم المسبب على السبب فإن الشراء سبب الملك والملك مسبب فالمجاز صحيح. ففي هذه الصورة يصدق ديانةً وقضاءً لأن في صورة إن ملکت لا يعتق النصف الثاني، وفي صورة إن اشتریت يعتق، فقصد الأغلظ والأشد فلا يتهم بالكذب. وأما في قوله إن اشتریت فقال عنيت بالشراء الملك بطريق إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب فيصدق ديانةً لا قضاءً لأنه قصد التخفيف حيث إن في صورة الشراء يعتق وفي صورة الملك لا يعتق، فيتهم بالكذب. وذلك لأن الشراء سبب مشروعيته أن يكون علة لحكم الملك، لأن المقصود من الشراء الملك، فالمجاز جار من الطرفين. وهذا لا يؤثر على صحة الاستعارة.
For if he says 'I meant one by the other', i.e., he says 'I meant by ownership purchase, or by purchase ownership', he is believed in religious terms, not in court, where there is a reduction in his favor. That is, in the case of 'if I owned a slave', he says 'I meant by ownership the purchase' by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause, because purchase is the cause of ownership and ownership is the effect, so the metaphor is valid. In this case, he is believed both religiously and in court because in the case of 'if I owned', the second half is not freed, and in the case of 'if I bought', it is freed, so he intended the more severe and stronger (meaning) and thus is not accused of lying. As for his saying 'if I bought', and he says 'I meant by purchase ownership' by way of applying the name of the cause to the effect, then he is believed religiously, not in court, because he intended reduction: in the case of purchase it is freed, in the case of ownership it is not freed, so he is accused of lying. That is because purchase is a cause whose legislated purpose is to be the cause for the ruling of ownership, because the objective of purchase is ownership, thus the metaphor runs from both sides. And this does not affect the validity of the isti'arah.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi rule of tasdiq (believing a person's claim) when someone makes a statement using a word that can be interpreted metaphorically. The scenario involves a person who says 'if I own a slave' (in milktu) or 'if I buy a slave' (in ishtaraytu) as a condition for emancipation. If he later claims he meant the other form (e.g., he said 'if I own' but meant 'if I buy'), he is believed diyanatan (religiously, before Allah) in all cases. However, qada'an (in court) he is believed only if his interpretation leads to a stricter result (tasdid — making the ruling more severe) for himself, because then he is not suspected of lying to gain benefit. If the interpretation leads to a lighter result (takhfif), he is not believed in court because he is suspected of lying to avoid the stricter consequence. In the example: 'in milktu' leads to no emancipation of the second half (stricter for him if he intended emancipation), 'in ishtaraytu' leads to emancipation (lighter). So if he says 'in milktu' but claims he meant 'ishtaraytu', his interpretation is stricter (he intends to free the slave), so he is believed both in court and religiously. Conversely, if he says 'in ishtaraytu' but claims he meant 'milktu', his interpretation is lighter (he avoids emancipation), so he is believed only religiously, not in court. The passage also notes that this metaphorical usage (cause for effect or vice versa) is valid, and the court's non-acceptance does not affect the linguistic validity.

Key Terms
تصدیق (tasdiq): Acceptance of a person's claim regarding his intended meaning in a legal context.
دیانتہ (diyanatan): Religiously, based on the person's truthfulness before Allah, irrespective of court evidence.
قضاءً (qada'an): In court, based on apparent evidence and subject to judicial discretion.
استعارة (isti'arah): Metaphorical usage of a word in a transferred sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person vows to free his slave 'if I buy a slave' (in ishtaraytu abdan), then later claims he meant 'if I own a slave' (in milktu abdan). How is his claim treated?
A) Accepted both diyanatan and qada'an.
B) Accepted only diyanatan, not qada'an.
C) Accepted only qada'an, not diyanatan.
D) Not accepted at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a person's claim of intended meaning is always accepted before Allah, but in court it is accepted only if the claimed interpretation imposes a stricter ruling on the speaker.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Majaz as Successor: Speech vs. Judgment
Explains dispute over majaz being successor to haqiqah in speech or judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on the nature of majaz as successor to haqiqah and apply them to a concrete legal case.

Core Idea
Majaz is a successor to haqiqah, but the dispute is: successor in speech (Abu Hanifa) or successor in judgment (Sahibayn).

This passage delves into a key difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the function of metaphorical language in legal derivation. While all agree that majaz (metaphorical usage) stands in place of haqiqah (literal usage), they differ on the aspect of substitution: is it a substitution in the speech itself, or a substitution in the ruling derived from the speech? Understanding this nuance clarifies how each scholar evaluates examples like a master saying 'this is my son' to an older slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus and point of disagreement
Recognize that all scholars agree majaz is a successor to haqiqah, but differ in the aspect (jihah) of succession: speech or judgment.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's view: succession in speech (haqq al-takallum)
Abu Hanifa holds that the metaphorical expression replaces the literal one linguistically; grammatical correctness suffices for validity, even if the literal meaning is impossible in reality.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's view: succession in judgment (haqq al-hukm)
The Sahibayn hold that the metaphorical ruling is a weak substitute for the literal ruling; if the literal ruling is impossible, the metaphorical one cannot stand.
4
Apply the difference to the slave example
Master says 'hādhā ibnī' to an older slave. For Abu Hanifa, the speech is grammatically correct, so the slave becomes free. For the Sahibayn, since literal sonhood is impossible, the metaphorical freedom is invalid, so the slave is not freed.
Even though the speech is grammatically sound, the Sahibayn focus on the impossibility of the original ruling.
5
Recognize the condition for succession
For the Sahibayn, the original (haqiqah) must be possible in itself; if impossible due to an obstacle (like age), succession is blocked. For Abu Hanifa, only grammatical correctness is needed.
This condition explains the practical difference in rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة . المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة في حق التكلم عند ابي حنيفة ? اس میں کوئی اختلاف نہیں ہے کہ مجاز خلیفہ ہے حقیقتہ کا اور حقیقتہ اصل کے درجہ میں ہے برائے مجاز البتہ جہتہ خلفیہ میں اختلاف ہے کہ مجاز کس جہہ سے خلیفہ حقیقت ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مجاز خلیفہ ہے حقیقت کا فی حق التظلم اور صاحبین کے نزدیک خلیفہ ہے فی حق الحکم بندہ امام کے نزدیک کسی ایسے غلام کو جو اپنے سردار سے عمرمیں بڑا ہے۔ سردار کہتا ہے بہلا بنی تو اثبات حریقہ کے بارہ میں یہ خلف ہوگا اس تکلم سے کہ وہ ہذا اپنی اثبات نبوۃ کے بارہ میں کہہ والتكلم بالاصل صحيح اصل حقیقہ یعنی ہذا اپنی بفرص اثبات بنوۃ کے ساتھ تکلم کرنا بقانون عربیہ صحیح ہے۔ من حيث انه کیونکہ بایں حیثیت که لفظ ندا مبتدا ہے اور اپنی اس کی خبر ہے ۔ ترکیبی حیثیت سے قانونی سقم کوئی نہیں ہے۔ تو جب تکلم بالحقیقہ صحیح ہوا تو مجاز جو حقیقت کا خلف فی التکلم تھا وہ بھی صحیح ہو جائے گا ۔ تو اس کا مجازی معنی ہزارہ ثابت ہو جائیگا کیونکہ حریتہ لازم بنوا ہے ۔ وعندهما شيرت الحريه بهذا اللفظ تو اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ہذا اپنی سے ثبوت حریقہ خلف ہے اس کا کہ لفظ کے ساتھ ثبوت بنوہ ہو سکے ۔ والاصل ممتنع اصل یعنی اس لفظ کے ساتھ ثبوت بنوۃ ممتنع ہے کیونکہ جو متکلم سے عمر میں بڑا ہے ۔ وہ کسی صورت میں متکلم کا ابن نہیں ہو سکتا جب اصل ممکن نہ ہوا تو فرع یعنی مجاز بھی صحیح نہیں ہو گا لہذا حریتہ ثابت نہیں ہوگی ۔ ہاں اگر غلام چھوٹی عمر کا ہو جس کا ابن مولیٰ ہونا ممکن ہو سکتا تو پھر حریتہ ثابت ہو سکتی ہے ۔
و من شرط الخلف اور شرط خلف میں سے یہ ضروری ہے کہ اصل فی حد ذاتہ ممکن ہو اور اس اصل کا عدم ثبوت کسی عارض کی وجہ سے ہو. جیسے کہ سردار ایسے غلام کو جو معروف النسب ہے اور اپنے سردار سے عمر میں اتنا چھوٹا ہو کہ سردار سے اس عمر کا بیٹا ہونا صحیح ہو اس کو ہذا اپنی کہہ اور مراد اس کی حریتہ ہو ۔ تو یہ صحیح ہے آزاد ہو جائیگا۔ اور جو عمر میں بڑا ہے چونکہ اس کے اندر حقیقی مفہوم نا ممکن ہے ۔ اس لئے مجاز عن الحریہ صحیح نہیں ہو فيعتق عنده - اى عند الامام کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک مجاز خلف فی التکلم تھا اور ہذا اپنی اکبر سنا کو کہنا کلام عربیہ کے
کے لحاظ سے صحیح ہے کہ حسب ضابط یہ مبتدا و خبر ہے لہذا مجاز بطور خلف کے مراد لینا صحیح ہوگا۔
اتفق العلماء ان المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة آلخم یعنی علماء اس امریم متفق ہیں کہ مجاز حقیقہ کا خلف اور شریع ہے۔ ثم اختلفوا في ان الخلفية پھر اس میں اختلاف ہے کہ یہ خلفیہ بحت تکلم ہے یا سکہ حکم ہے ۔ فعندهما في حق الحكم صاحبین کے نزدیک فی حق الحکم ہے یعنی جو حکم اس لفظ کے ساتھ بطریق مجازہ ثابت ہو وہ ضعف ہے اس حکم کا جو اس لفظ کے ساتھ بطریق حقیقہ ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ متل الفقہ بنا امنی کے ساتھ جو حکم حریہ ابتراق مجانہ ثابت ہوتا ہے. بی خیلف ہے اس حکم کا جو اس لفظ کے ساتھ بشاریق حقیقہ ثابت ہوتا ہے یعنی ثبوت بنوہ خلاصہ یہ کہ ندا اپنی سے مفہوم حریتہ مراد لینا یہ ضیفہ ہے۔ ندا اپنی کے مفہوم اہلیہ کا اور مجاز اس وقت صحیح ہوگا ۔ جب حقیقہ کا امریتا
ہو گا اور اکبر سنا میں ثبوت ہے ناممکن ہے ۔ منا مجاز صحیح نہیں واہ وعند ابي حنيفة في حق التكلم امام نزدیک جو خلیفہ فی حق التکم ہے اس کی تفسیر میں کچھ اختلاف ہے ۔ تبعض الشارحین ضروب الا بعض شارحین نے اس کی تفسیریوں کی ہے کہ لفظ بدر بنی جب اس سے مراد حریتہ ہو تو یہ خلیفہ ہے ۔ لفظ نذاحر کا نو گو یا کہ اس لفظ کے ساتھ تکلم کرنا جو اس معنی حریتہ کا بطور مجاز کے مفید ہے یعنی بدامنی یہ خلیفہ ہے اس لفظ کے تنظیم کا۔ جو اس معنی حریہ کا مفید ہے بحریق حقیقتہ کے یعنی بابا جی کا تو گے یا ندانی و تسلم یہ خلیفہ ہوا مداح کے ساتھ حرہ تکلم کرنے کا . وبعضهم نسروه بان لفظ هد : ابنى اذا اريد بن الحرية يعني بعض حضرات نے اس کی تفسیر یوں کی : ہے کہ غلط بدا اپنی جب اس سے مراد حریقہ ہو تو یہ مجاز خلیفہ ہے ، غلط ہذا اپنی کا جب اس سے مراد نبوۃ ہو تو اس تفسیر کے ہی ظ سے حل ہو ا لفظ بدا اپنی جو اثبات بنوۃ کے لئے ہے اور خلف ہوا ندا اپنی جس سے مراد هریه بود الوحد الاول صحيح فهد المدنى یعنی وجہ اول اس معنی حریقہ کے بارہ میں نیچے ہے اور مفید عرض بھی، کیونکہ اس کلام سے معرض اثبات خریہ ہے تو اس بارہ میں لفظ بذاحیہ اصل ہے اور لفظا بھی صحیح ہے اور حکم بھی صحیح ہے لہذا ہر اپنی کا لفظ برا دریا کے لفظ کا قائم مقام ہوگا ۔ لہذا مجاز کا خلف ہونا صحیح ہو جائے گا۔ والوجد الثاني اليق بهذا المقام لیکن تفسیر کی وجہ ثانی اس مقام کے ساتھ زیادہ لائق اور مناسب ہے ۔ لامرین اس کے الیق مونیکی دو وجہیں ہیں ۔ احد هما ایک یہ کہ امام اور صاحبین کے مابین اس امر پر اتفاق ہے کہ مجاز خلف عن الحقیقۃ ہے اسمیں کوئی اختلاف نہیں البتہ علما یہ ذکر کرتے ہیں کہ ان کے مابین اختلاف جہتہ خلفیہ میں ہے۔ نیجب ان لا يكون الخ پس واجب ہے کہ اختلاف اس میں نہ ہو کہ اصل کون ہے اور خلف کون ہے بلکہ ہر ایک فریق کے نزدیک اصل بھی ایک ہو اور خلف بھی ایک ہو۔ بل الخلاف بلکہ خلاف صرف جہتہ خلفیہ میں ہو نہ کہ تعیین اصل و خلف کے مجموعہ ہیں ۔ فعندھما پس صاحبین کے نزدیک مذا ابنی جب کہ مجاز ہو یعنی اس سے معنی حریتہ مراد ہو تو خلف عن هذا ابني جب کہ یہ حقیقہ ہو یعنی اس سے مراد اثبات بنوۃ ہو اور یہ خلفیہ بحق حکم ہے۔ یعنی
Issue: Majaz is a successor to Haqiqah in the right of speech according to Abu Hanifa? There is no disagreement that Majaz is a successor to Haqiqah and Haqiqah is in the rank of the original for Majaz. However, there is disagreement in the aspect of succession: from which aspect is Majaz a successor to Haqiqah. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, Majaz is a successor to Haqiqah in the right of speech (haqq al-takallum), and according to the Sahibayn, it is a successor in the right of judgment (haqq al-hukm). Example: According to the Imam, for a slave who is older than his master, the master says 'this is my son' intending to declare his freedom; this is a succession from the speech that says 'this is my son' for establishing sonhood. The speech with the original (haqiqah) is correct, i.e., saying 'this is my son' by way of establishing sonhood according to Arabic grammar is correct. Because in this respect, the word 'hādha' is the subject and 'ibnī' is the predicate. There is no grammatical defect. So when the speech with haqiqah is correct, then the majaz which is a successor to haqiqah in speech also becomes correct. So the metaphorical meaning (freedom) is established because freedom is a necessary consequence of sonhood. According to them, establishing freedom with this word is a successor to the word establishing sonhood. The original is impossible: establishing sonhood with this word is impossible because one who is older than the speaker cannot be the speaker's son in any case. When the original is not possible, the branch (majaz) also does not become correct. Hence freedom is not established. Yes, if the slave is of young age such that it is possible for him to be the son of the master, then freedom can be established. And from the conditions of succession: it is necessary that the original be possible in itself, and its non-establishment be due to some obstacle. For example, if the master says 'this is my son' to a slave whose lineage is known and who is so much younger than his master that it is plausible for him to be his son, and he intends his freedom, then this is correct; he will become free. But for the one who is older, since the literal meaning is impossible in his case, therefore majaz for freedom is not correct. So he is not freed according to the Imam, because in his view majaz is successor in speech, and saying 'this is my son' to an older person is correct from the perspective of Arabic grammar because grammatically it is subject and predicate, so taking majaz as a successor is correct. The scholars unanimously agree that Majaz is a successor to Haqiqah. Then they disagreed whether the succession pertains to speech or judgment. According to the Sahibayn, it is in the right of judgment: i.e., the judgment established by the word via majaz is a weak successor to the judgment established by the word via haqiqah. For example, with 'this is my son' the judgment of freedom established via majaz is a successor to the judgment of sonhood established via haqiqah. In summary, taking the meaning of freedom from 'this is my son' is a weak successor. Majaz is correct only when the haqiqah is possible, but in the older person, it is impossible, so majaz is not correct. According to Abu Hanifa, it is in the right of speech. There is some difference in interpretation among the commentators. Some interpreted that the word 'ibnī' when intended for freedom is a successor to the word 'ibnī' for sonhood. Others interpreted that the word 'hādhā ibnī' when intended for freedom is a successor to the word 'hādhā ibnī' when intended for sonhood. The first interpretation is correct in terms of meaning because the speech is contextually about establishing freedom, so the word 'ibnī' is the original and is correct in word and judgment, so 'hādhā ibnī' stands in place of the word for freeing. Therefore, the succession of majaz is correct. However, the second interpretation is more appropriate for this context for two reasons. One is that there is agreement between the Imam and the Sahibayn that majaz is a successor to haqiqah; there is no dispute in that. Rather, scholars mention that the disagreement is in the aspect of succession. So it is necessary that the disagreement not be about which is the original and which is the successor, but rather each party agrees on the same original and successor, and the disagreement is only in the aspect of succession, not in the determination of original and successor collectively. So according to the Sahibayn, 'hādhā ibnī' when used metaphorically (intending freedom) is a successor to 'hādhā ibnī' when used literally (intending sonhood), and this succession is in the right of judgment.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a classical usul dispute regarding the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) as a successor (khalif) to haqiqah (literal meaning). The author first establishes the consensus that majaz is indeed a successor to haqiqah. The disagreement is about the aspect of succession: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that majaz is a successor in the right of speech (haqq al-takallum), meaning that the metaphorical expression stands in place of the literal one linguistically. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that it is a successor in the right of judgment (haqq al-hukm), meaning that the ruling derived from the metaphor is a weaker substitute for the ruling from the literal. The text illustrates this with the example of a master saying 'hādhā ibnī' (this is my son) to an older slave, intending to declare his freedom. According to Abu Hanifa, because the literal statement 'this is my son' is grammatically correct and possible (if the slave were young), the metaphorical use for freedom is also correct when addressed to an older slave, since the grammatical structure is sound. However, the Sahibayn argue that since the literal meaning (sonhood) is impossible for an older slave, the original haqiqah is impossible; thus the majaz cannot be a valid successor, and the slave does not become free. The passage then discusses conditions for succession: the original must be possible in itself, and its non-occurrence due to an obstacle. The author also reviews different interpretations by commentators regarding how to define the succession. The preferred interpretation is that the disagreement is only in the aspect (jihah) of succession, not in identifying which expression is original and which is successor. This maintains that both parties agree on the same linguistic pair (hādhā ibnī for sonhood as original, and for freedom as metaphor) but differ on whether the succession is in speech or judgment.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used in a meaning other than its original literal meaning due to a relationship and an indicator.
خلف (khalaf): A successor that takes the place of an original (haqiqah) in usage, either in speech or in judgment.
حق التكلم (haqq al-takallum): The right of speech: the validity of the expression as a correct Arabic utterance.
حق الحكم (haqq al-hukm): The right of judgment: the validity of the ruling derived from the expression being a substitute for the literal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does a master's statement 'hādhā ibnī' to an older slave not establish the slave's freedom?
A) Because the speech is grammatically incorrect.
B) Because the literal meaning (sonhood) is impossible, so the metaphorical ruling cannot be a valid successor.
C) Because Abu Hanifa's view is not followed.
D) Because the master did not intend freedom.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal disagreement on majaz succession (speech vs. judgment) affects practical rulings such as the effect of a metaphorical declaration of manumission.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Condition of Haqiqi Possibility for Majaz
Explains the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on majaz validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the condition of haqiqi meaning for majaz, evaluate the Sahibayn's proof from oath analogy, and apply the ruling to the case of calling a slave 'ibn'.

Core Idea
For majaz, only understanding the haqiqi meaning is required; its possibility is not.

This lesson explores a key usul dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Sahibayn) regarding the validity of metaphorical usage (majaz). At stake is whether the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word must be possible for the metaphor to be valid. The Sahibayn argue from the nature of substitution (khalaf) and analogy with oaths, while Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between understanding (fahm) and intention (irada). The practical difference emerges vividly in the case of calling a known slave 'ibn' (son).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf
Locate the point of disagreement: when a word is used in a metaphorical sense, is the possibility of its real meaning a condition? State each position clearly.
Imam Abu Hanifa: possibility not required; Sahibayn: required.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
Follow their reasoning: majaz is a transfer from haqiqi to lazim; the branch requires the root to be possible. They support this with the oath analogy where expiation (khalaf) requires the possibility of the original act (birr). Study the examples of touching the sky and drinking water.
The analogy shows that where the original is impossible, the substitute fails.
3
Analyse Imam Abu Hanifa's response
He argues that transfer depends on understanding (fahm) the haqiqi, not intending it. Since one cannot intend both haqiqi and majazi simultaneously, only understanding is relevant. As long as the word is grammatically correct and the haqiqi is understandable, majaz is valid.
The key distinction is between fahm (understanding) and irada (intention).
4
Apply to the legal case
Consider calling a known slave 'ibn'. The haqiqi meaning (biological son) is impossible to intend, but it is understood. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the metaphorical meaning (freedom) is intended, so the utterance is an acknowledgment of emancipation. According to the Sahibayn, the majaz is invalid and no legal effect follows.
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: does the slave become free or not?

ہوا اور یہی عشق بنوة کا لازم ہے ۔ فیجعل اقراراً پس یہ لفظ ہذا اپنی اقرار بالعتق ہوگا اور یہ غلام بلانیتہ قضاء ازار ہو جائیں گا ۔ لانہ متعین کیونکہ یہی معنی عشق اس وقت میں ہے اور اس کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے
And the possibility of acting upon the real meaning—these two are specific to the word 'this son' and not to other words, because that (other word) is absolutely correct and acting upon its real meaning is not impossible. So it is learned that the root (asl) is the word 'ibn'. When prophecy is intended from it, it becomes subject and predicate, and acting upon the real meaning is impossible. So the conclusion of the disagreement between the two parties is: when a word is used and a metaphorical meaning is intended, is the possibility of its real meaning a condition or not? According to them (the two companions), the possibility of the real meaning is a condition. So where the real meaning is impossible, according to them metaphor is not valid. So when 'this son' is said to the eldest (akbar sina), the real meaning is absolutely impossible, so intending the metaphorical meaning—i.e., freedom—is not valid. According to him (Imam Abu Hanifa), the possibility of the real meaning is not a condition for the validity of metaphor; rather, only the correctness of the word from the perspective of Arabic grammar suffices. The proof of the two companions is that in metaphor there is a transfer of the mind from the literal meaning to its concomitant. The second (the concomitant) depends on the first (the literal meaning), which is like the entailed (malzum). So the concomitant, which is metaphor, is a substitute and branch of the literal meaning. Therefore, the possibility of the first is necessary. Based on this is the issue of touching the sky: the possibility of the root is a condition for the validity of the substitute. The form of the issue: a person swears, 'By God, I will surely touch the sky.' Then expiation becomes immediately obligatory, because expiation is a substitute (khalaf) and fulfilling the oath is the root. So wherever fulfilling the oath is not possible, the oath is not contracted and expiation is not obligatory. In the issue of touching the sky, fulfilling—i.e., actually touching the sky—is possible for a human being, as was for the Prophet on the night of Mi'raj. Therefore this oath is contracted; since the swearer cannot actually touch it, he breaks the oath and expiation becomes obligatory. And if one swears, 'I will drink the water that is in this jug,' while there is no water in the jug, then expiation is not obligatory because fulfilling the oath, which is the root, is impossible at the time of the oath. So the oath is not contracted and no expiation is obligatory. So these two issues—touching the sky and drinking water—and the distinction made between them serve as an example for this issue: in 'akbar sina' the root is impossible, so freeing does not occur; in 'asghar sina' it is possible, so he is freed. In the text (Tanqih) the issue of the jug is not mentioned, only the issue of touching the sky, because in our books both are commonly and famously mentioned together, so mentioning one suffices. We say: it depends on understanding the first. This is the response to the proof of the two companions: the transfer from the first to the second depends on understanding the first, not on intending it. Because it is not possible to combine intention between the real and the metaphorical meaning. The summary of the response: the transfer from the literal meaning to the metaphorical depends only on the understanding of the real meaning—i.e., the words should be such that the real meaning can be understood from them, without any ambiguity—and does not depend on the possibility of intending the real meaning. So when the transfer does not depend on intending the first, the possibility of the first is not necessary. It would only be necessary if intending the first were necessary. And since it depends on understanding the first, and understanding the first only depends on the word being correct from the perspective of Arabic grammar, then for the transfer to metaphor, the speech being correct from the Arabic perspective is sufficient, and correctness in meaning and possibility are not needed. And when the words permit understanding the real meaning but it cannot be intended—whether because the slave is known to be of free lineage or because he is older than the master—it will be known that what is intended is its concomitant, and the concomitant of 'ibn' is freedom (itq), so freedom is intended. From the time he owned him, it becomes an acknowledgment of emancipation, and the slave is freed without the slave's intention (niyyah), because this meaning is determined at that time and not another.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on the condition for the validity of majaz (metaphorical usage). The author explains the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whether the haqiqi (real) meaning must be possible for the majaz to be valid. The two companions argue that since majaz involves a transfer from the real to the metaphorical, the real meaning must be possible because the metaphorical is a branch (far') of the real (asl). They support this with examples from oaths: one who swears to touch the sky must pay expiation because fulfilling the oath is impossible (the real meaning of touching the sky is possible in itself but not for the swearer), while one who swears to drink water from a jug with no water does not incur expiation because the real act (drinking the water) is impossible from the start. Imam Abu Hanifa responds that the transfer depends only on understanding the real meaning, not on intending it. Since it is impossible to intend both the real and metaphorical meanings simultaneously, only understanding is required. As long as the word is grammatically correct such that the real meaning can be understood, the metaphorical usage is valid. This principle is then applied to the case of calling a known slave 'ibn' (son): since the real meaning (biological son) is understood but cannot be intended (because the slave is known to be not a son), the metaphorical meaning—freedom—is intended, and thus the utterance becomes an acknowledgment of the slave's freedom, freeing him. The passage thus clarifies Hanafi usul on majaz and its application to legal rulings.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqat): The original, literal meaning of a word as coined
مجاز (majaz): Use of a word in a meaning other than its original, due to a relationship and a contextual clue (qarinah)
خلف (khalaf): Substitute or replacement; in oaths, expiation is a substitute for fulfilling the oath
لازم (lazim): Concomitant; the meaning that necessarily accompanies the original meaning (e.g., freedom is concomitant of sonship in the context of a slave)
فہم (fahm): Understanding; the mental comprehension of a meaning
ارادہ (irada): Intention; the will to denote a specific meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position as explained in this passage, what is the minimum condition for a metaphorical usage (majaz) to be valid?
A) The haqiqi meaning must be possible in reality.
B) The word must be grammatically correct and the haqiqi meaning must be understandable.
C) The speaker must intend the haqiqi meaning.
D) The listener must accept the metaphorical meaning.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the validity of majaz requires only that the haqiqi meaning be understandable and the word grammatically correct; the possibility of intending the haqiqi is not a condition.

The Vow of Fasting in Rajab: Necessary Implication vs. Majaz
Resolving the issue of combining literal and metaphorical in a vow intended as an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school's position that a vow with oath intention is both a vow and an oath does not require combining literal and metaphorical meaning, and apply the concept of iltizam (necessary implication) to distinguish it from majaz.

Core Idea
The oath is a necessary implication of the vow, not a metaphorical meaning, so no combining of literal and metaphorical occurs.

In this passage, a classic usul dispute is examined: when someone says 'Upon me is the fast of Rajab' with the intention of making an oath, does this create a combining of literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) meanings? Abu Yusuf says only an oath, while Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say both a vow and an oath. The objection is that this combines literal and metaphorical. The Hanafi response refines this by arguing that the oath is not a metaphorical meaning but a necessary implication (iltizam) of the vow, similar to how the word 'lion' implies courage. This lesson unpacks this argument and its usul significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and its parties
State the positions of Abu Yusuf vs. the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) regarding the utterance.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that the two's position requires combining literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) in one utterance.
3
Analyze the first response
The response: the vow is literal by form, and the oath is an implied effect (muwjib) not a metaphorical meaning.
4
Distinguish iltizam from majaz
Using the example of 'asad' (lion) implying courage, see how necessary implication is not majaz.
5
Address the further objection about intention
The second answer: vow is established without intention by form, oath requires intention, so no combining in intention occurs.

And the statement to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) is also an objection of combining literal and metaphorical. And its answer is: Yes, someone said... When a person says 'Upon me is the fast of Rajab' and intends by it an oath, then according to the two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), according to their statement, it is a vow and an oath: it is a vow by form and also an oath by effect. In this issue there are different scenarios mentioned in al-Talwih. One scenario: the speaker intends only an oath; according to Imam Abu Yusuf, it is only an oath, and according to the two, it is both a vow and an oath. Even if he does not intend? If that person does not fast in Rajab, then due to the vow, fulfillment is required; upon missing, only expiation (kaffarah) is necessary. Now since he did not perform the fast of Rajab, due to the vow, qada (make-up) is required, and due to the oath, kaffarah is required. According to Abu Yusuf, only kaffarah is required, which is the requirement of the oath, and qada is not required, which is the requirement of the vow. This is the practical consequence of the dispute. And if it is a vow, then when it is both a vow and an oath, there is a combining of literal and metaphorical because the word is literal in vow and metaphorical in oath. Because it is a vow by its form? This is the proof? And the answer to the objection is: In summary, the answer is that it is a vow in terms of its form, and in terms of its necessary effect and requirement, it is an oath. You have established that it is an oath by its requirement... In summary, it is that making a permissible thing obligatory (ijab mubah) is to make a permissible thing obligatory upon oneself, as here he made the fast of Rajab obligatory. It requires the prohibition of its opposite (yujib tahrim diddih): it necessitates the prohibition of the opposite of this permissible thing, like in this context, abandoning the fast of Rajab becomes prohibited. The prohibition of the permissible (tahrim al-halal) is an oath. And forbidding a permissible thing upon oneself is an oath. Since for this person, during the days of Rajab, not fasting and eating and drinking were permissible, with this vow he forbade these things upon himself, so it becomes an oath. Just as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade honey upon himself, then Allah said: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (Quran 66:2). So He considered that prohibition an oath. Similarly, the purchase of a relative is a form of purchase, but in terms of its effect, it is emancipation: the close relative who is a mahram becomes free from servitude. So the conclusion of the answer is that this is not combining literal and metaphorical, but rather these words in terms of their form are posited for vow, and the required effect (muwjib) of this speech is a necessary implication (lazim muta'akhkhir). That is, it has a literal meaning, and the oath is an implied meaning (iltizami), and the indication of a word to its necessary implication is not majaz. For example, the word 'asad' (lion) when intended for the specific predatory animal, its courage which is its necessary implication indicates it by way of implication (tariq al-iltizam), and this is not majaz. Because majaz is when the intended meaning is the necessary implication of the word's literal meaning without intending the literal meaning. Here it is not like that. Therefore, combining literal and metaphorical is not necessary. Then an objection occurred to my mind: that if this is an oath by its implied effect, then whenever the effect of this speech is an oath, it should become an oath even if he does not intend it, because the effect of speech is established without intention. Like when one purchases a relative, its effect (emancipation) is established even if he does not intend it. But here it is not like that; rather, an oath is established with intention and will. And if the oath is not an effect of this speech, then combining literal and metaphorical would be necessary. It can be said in answer to this objection... The answer is: Here there is no combining of literal and metaphorical in terms of intention. Because he intended the oath and did not intend the vow, so there is no combining in intention. However, the vow is established by its form, which does not require intention, so it is established without intention. And the oath is established with intention. Because the speech is posited for the vow, and it is a performative utterance (insha). So its literal meaning is established even without intention, and the oath was established with intention, so no combining in intention occurred. The reality of this answer: In this answer, it is admitted that the oath is a metaphorical meaning of this speech. In the first answer, it was considered a necessary implication and implied meaning, but in performative utterances, it is possible...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This lengthy passage addresses a classic usul dispute about whether saying 'Upon me is the fast of Rajab' with the intention of making an oath results in a combination of literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) meanings in one utterance. The dispute is between Abu Yusuf, who says it is only an oath, and Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who say it is both a vow and an oath. The practical consequence is that if he fails to fast, Abu Yusuf requires only kaffarah (expiation), while the two require both qada (make-up for the vow) and kaffarah (for the oath). The accusation is that the two's position necessitates combining literal and metaphorical meanings. The response is that the word in its form is a vow (literal), but its legal effect is an oath (metaphorical or implied). The author refines this by arguing that the oath is not a metaphorical meaning but rather a necessary implication (lazim) of the vow. Using the analogy of the word 'asad' (lion) which implies courage, the oath is implied by the vow just as courage is implied by lion. This is not majaz because the literal meaning is still intended. A further objection arises: if the oath is an effect, it should be established without intention, but it requires intention. The final answer distinguishes between the literal meaning (vow) which is established without intention by the form, and the implied meaning (oath) which requires intention. Thus no combining in intention occurs. The author notes that this second answer concedes that the oath is metaphorical, but that it is permissible in performative utterances. This reflects the Hanafi usul concept of 'iltizam' (necessary implication) versus 'majaz'.

Key Terms
نذر (nazr): A vow; a commitment to perform a permissible act of worship.
یمین (yamin): An oath; a declaration that makes a permissible thing prohibited upon oneself.
التزامی (iltizami): Implied meaning; a meaning conveyed through necessary implication of the literal meaning.
موجب (muwjib): The legal effect or requirement of an utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Abu Yusuf requires qada; the two require kaffarah
B) Abu Yusuf requires only kaffarah; the two require both qada and kaffarah
C) Abu Yusuf considers it a vow; the two consider it an oath
D) Abu Yusuf requires nothing; the two require both

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the apparent combining of literal and metaphorical by treating the oath as a necessary implication (iltizam) of the vow, a distinct category from majaz.

The Conjunction Waw in Hanafi Usul: Absolute Conjunction, Not Order
Explaining the Hanafi view that waw is for absolute conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the conjunction waw indicates absolute conjunction and apply it to the wudu verse to derive that order of limbs is not obligatory.

Core Idea
The waw indicates absolute conjunction, not order; order requires external evidence.

This passage introduces a fundamental usul debate: does the Arabic conjunction waw imply sequence? The Hanafi school, relying on linguistic induction and key examples, maintains that waw merely joins without ordering. Understanding this principle directly affects rulings on acts like wudu, where the waw between limbs is used. In this lesson, we will examine the Hanafi position and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Claim
The author states that waw is for absolute conjunction (mujarrad al-'atf) and does not indicate order. Note that this is the relied-upon view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
This position is supported by language scholars and inductive reasoning.
2
Examine the Linguistic Evidence
The passage cites the example 'لا تأكل السمك وتشرب اللبن' (do not eat fish and drink milk). The waw here joins two actions without specifying order; the prohibition applies to both possible sequences. This illustrates that waw is for mere conjunction.
The dual alif analogy is also mentioned: waw between different nouns functions like the dual alif between identical nouns.
3
Apply the Principle to the Wudu Verse
The author concludes that since the wudu verse uses waw between the limbs (e.g., 'wash your faces and hands'), order is not obligatory. This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the debate.
This contrasts with schools that require order in wudu, who interpret the waw as implying sequence.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Variant
The passage mentions that some attribute to Abu Hanifa the view that waw indicates order, while the Sahibayn hold the absolute conjunction view. The text ultimately prefers the Sahibayn's position.
This intra-madhhab difference is a nuance; the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

کے لئے ہے۔ بالنقل عن ائمة اللغة ایک تو ائمہ لغہ سے سے ایسے منقول ہے داستقراء مواضع استعمالها اور اس کے مقام استعمال کا استقراء بھی اسی پر دال ہے کہ یہ مطلق عطف کے لئے ہے وھی بین الاسمين المختلفين یعنی لئے واو عطف استعمال کی گئی ہے۔ وقولہم لا تاكل السمك ونشرب اللبن یہاں بھی مقصد یہی ہے۔ که لا تجمع بينهما خواہ مچھلی پہلے اور دودھ بعد میں یا اس کا بر عکس ہو دونوں ممنوع میں ترتیب نہیں ہے نلهذا لا يجب الترتیب چونکہ ایتہ وضور میں اعضاء ار ہو کے ادا کرنے کے حکم میں واو عطف استعمال ہوتی ہے جو مطلق اجتماع کو چاہتی ہے اس لئے ترتیب واجب نہیں ہے ۔ دامانی السعی یہ جواب ہے ان حضرات کے استدلال کا جو واؤ کے برائے ترتیب ہونے کے لئے پیش کرتے تھے استدلال یوں ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا ان الصفا والمروة من شعائر اللہ تو صحابہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہم نے کہا باتما نبد ہم کس کے ساتھ ابتدا کریں تو نبی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا ابدد ایما بدا اللہ اسی کے ساتھ ابتدا کہ وجس کے ساتھ اللہ تعالیٰ نے ابتدا کی ہے تو نبی کریم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے اسی آیت سے ترتیب سمجھی اور اسی کا حکم دیا تو معلوم ہوا کہ واو برائے ترتیب ہے خلاصہ جواب یہ کہ سعی بین الصفا و المروۃ کے اندر جو ترتیب ہے وہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے قول ابدوا بما بدا اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ ہے۔ نہ کہ قرآن مجید کے ساتھ کیونکہ ان کا شعائر اللہ سے ہونا اس ترتیب کا متقاضی نہیں ہے ۔ وقولہ علیہ السلام ابد وا بما بدا الله تعالی بھی اس پر دال نہیں ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے جو ابتدا بالصفا فرمائی ہے۔ یہی تمہاری ہدایتہ کا موجب ہے لکن تقدیمہ لیکن جو صفا کو قرآن مجید میں مقد ما ذکر کیا گیا یہ کسی مصلحت سے خالی نہیں ہے مثلا تعظیم یا اس کی اہمیتہ وغیرہ ہو سکتی ہے اور یہ اہمیت اس کی اولویتہ کو تقاضا کرتی ہے نہ کہ وجوب کو البتہ وجوب ترتیب رسول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے لئے وحی غیر متلو سے ظاہر ہوئی اور ہمارے لئے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے قول ابدوا بما ابدا اللہ تعالیٰ سے بہر حال آیتہ جو مشتمل علی الود و العاطفہ ہے یہ موجب ترتیب نہیں ہے ۔ وزعم البعض انها للترتيب عند ابي حنيفة الخ بعض حضرات کا خیال ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک داو برائے ترتیب ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک برائے مقاز یقہ استدلالاً اپنے اس خیال پر بطور استدلال کے یہ مسئلہ پیش کرتے ہیں کہ جب ایک شخص اپنی بیوی غیر مدخولہ کو کہتا ہے
Let we mention here some particles which are greatly needed, and they are called particles of meaning. Among them are conjunctions. The waw is for absolute conjunction, i.e., it does not indicate order, but only absolute aggregation. This is transmitted from the imams of language, and the induction of its usage also indicates that it is for absolute conjunction between two different nouns, i.e., it gives the same benefit between two different nouns as the dual alif gives between two identical nouns. For example, as you say 'rajulan', similarly 'rajul wa imra'a' gives the same benefit. The dual alif is impossible between different nouns, so the waw of conjunction is used. And their saying 'La ta'kul al-samaka wa tashrab al-laban' (Do not eat fish and drink milk) also has the same purpose: that you do not combine them, whether fish first and milk later or vice versa, both are prohibited, no order is intended. Therefore, order is not obligatory. Since in the verse of wudu, in the ruling of performing the limbs, the waw of conjunction is used, which indicates absolute aggregation, so order is not obligatory. The argument of those who claim that waw indicates order: Allah said 'Indeed as-Safa and al-Marwa are among the symbols of Allah.' The Companions said, 'Which should we begin with?' The Prophet said, 'Begin with what Allah began.' So the Prophet understood order from the verse and ordered accordingly. Thus waw indicates order. The summary of the answer: The order in the Sa'i between Safa and Marwa is due to the Prophet's statement 'Begin with what Allah began', not due to the Quran, because their being symbols of Allah does not necessitate order. And his statement 'Begin with what Allah began' does not indicate that Allah's beginning with Safa is your guidance; rather, the mention of Safa first in the Quran is not without some benefit, e.g., glorification or importance, which recommends priority but does not make it obligatory. The obligation of order became evident to the Prophet through unrecited revelation (wahy ghair matlu), and for us, it is from the Prophet's statement 'Begin with what Allah began.' In any case, the verse that contains the waw of conjunction does not necessitate order. And some have claimed that according to Abu Hanifa, the waw indicates order, and according to the two companions (Sahibayn), it indicates absolute conjunction, using as evidence the case where a man says to his non-consummated wife...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary addresses the meaning of the conjunction waw. The author begins by stating that waw is for absolute conjunction (mujarrad al-'atf) and does not indicate order (tartib). This is supported by the statements of language scholars and by induction. The example of 'do not eat fish and drink milk' shows that the prohibition applies to combining them regardless of order. Similarly, in the wudu verse, since waw is used between the limbs, order is not obligatory in wudu according to the Hanafi view. The author then refutes the opposing argument that waw indicates order based on the verse about Safa and Marwa and the hadith 'Begin with what Allah began.' The response is that the order in Sa'i comes from the Prophet's command, not from the waw itself. The mention of Safa first in the Quran is for glorification, not to establish obligation. Finally, the passage mentions a claim that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, waw indicates order, while according to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), it indicates absolute conjunction. The narrator gives an example from divorce (cut off).

Key Terms
واو عطف (waw al-'atf): The conjunction particle waw, which indicates absolute conjunction (mujarrad al-jam') without order.
ترتیب (tartib): Order or sequence between actions or items; in this context, whether the waw implies a specific order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what does the conjunction waw primarily indicate?
A) Order (tartib) as a rule.
B) Absolute conjunction (mujarrad al-jam') without order.
C) Causation (ta'lil).
D) Negation (nafy).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the conjunction waw indicates absolute conjunction; any order must be established by separate evidence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

10 min
The Sa'i Argument and the Hanafi Refutation: Order from Sunnah, Not from Waw
Refuting the claim that waw indicates order using the Sa'i example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the opposing argument from Sa'i and explain why the Hanafi response does not concede that waw indicates order, and identify the intra-Hanafi variant attributed to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
The order in Sa'i comes from the hadith, not from the waw; the waw itself remains a mere conjunction.

A key challenge to the Hanafi view comes from the Quranic mention of Safa and Marwa and the Prophet's command to begin with what Allah began. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi refutation, distinguishing the role of the Quranic waw from the Prophetic command, and explores a reported difference within the Hanafi school itself. By the end, you will be able to articulate why the waw does not necessitate order and how the Sunnah provides the basis for sequence in Sa'i.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Opposing Argument from Sa'i
The opponents point to the verse 'إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائِرِ اللَّهِ' and the Prophet's command 'ابدؤوا بما بدأ الله' (Begin with what Allah began). They claim that the Prophet understood order from the waw in the verse, so waw indicates order.
This argument seems strong because it links a Quranic text with a Prophetic practice.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response has two prongs: (1) The order in Sa'i is solely due to the Prophet's statement, not the Quranic waw. (2) The Quranic mention of Safa first is for glorification (ta'zim) or importance, not to establish obligation.
The author emphasizes that 'كونهما من شعائر الله' (being symbols of Allah) does not imply order.
3
Examine the Role of the Prophet's Statement
The Prophet's command 'ابدؤوا بما بدأ الله' is an independent evidence (Sunnah) for order, not an interpretation of the waw. The waw itself remains a particle of absolute conjunction.
This shows that the Prophet's action can impose order even when the underlying particle does not.
4
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Difference
The passage notes that some attribute to Abu Hanifa the view that waw indicates order, while the Sahibayn hold the absolute conjunction view. This intra-madhhab ikhtilaf affects only the interpretation; the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's view.
This variation is often cited but the later Hanafi tradition prefers the Sahibayn.

The Hanafi Position: Waw al-‘Aṭf Indicates Mere Conjunction, Not Order
Dispute about meaning of waw al-‘aṭf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that waw al-‘aṭf indicates absolute conjunction (jama‘) and apply it to refute the claim that it implies order (tartib), using the linguistic and textual proofs presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Waw al-‘aṭf is for absolute conjunction; any apparent order comes from external indicators.

The simple conjunction waw (و) is a critical particle in Arabic legal language. Its interpretation determines whether acts like wudu require sequence or mere combination. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position that waw is for pure conjunction, not order, and examines the strongest counter-argument from the Safa and Marwa verse.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on waw
Identify the core claim: waw is for absolute conjunction (jama‘), not order (tartib).
This is based on transmission from language authorities and induction of usage.
2
Identify the linguistic proofs
Examine the examples given: the prohibition 'do not eat fish and drink milk' shows that combining is prohibited regardless of order; similar is the wudu verse where waw connects actions without implying sequence.
The wudu verse is a key fiqh application.
3
Analyze the opposing proof from Safa and Marwa
The opponent cites the Quranic verse (2:158) and the Prophet's command 'Begin with what Allah began' to argue that waw indicates order.
This is the strongest counter-argument.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The order in sa‘y comes from the Prophet's statement, not from the waw itself. The precedence of Safa in mention is due to importance (ta‘ẓīm), not obligation. Thus the waw in the verse remains for mere conjunction.
The response distinguishes between the particle's meaning and external instructions.

ذکر کرتے ہیں جن کی طرف حاجہ شدیدہ ہوتی ہے اور ان کا نام حروف معانی ہے ان میں سے حروف عطف ہیں الواد به مطلق عطف کے لئے ہے یعنی ترتیب پر دال نہیں ہے بلکہ مطلق جمع کے لئے ہے۔ بالنقل عن ائمة اللغة ایک تو ائمہ لغہ سے ایسے منقول ہے داستقراء مواضع استعمالها اور اس کے مقام استعمال کا استقراء بھی اسی پر دال ہے کہ یہ مطلق عطف کے لئے ہے وھی بین الاسمين المختلفين یعنی یہ واو اسمین مختلفین کے اندر وہی فائدہ دیتی ہے جو الف تثنیہ بین الاسمين المتحدين فائدہ دیتا مثلاً جیسے آپ کہیں رجلان تو اس جیسا فائدہ ہوگا رجل وامراة میں ان مختلفین میں الف تشنہ کالا ناممکن نہیں ہے اس لئے واو عطف استعمال کی گئی ہے۔ وقولہم لا تاكل السمك ونشرب اللبن یہاں بھی مقصد یہی ہے۔ که لا تجمع بينهما خواہ مچھلی پہلے اور دودھ بعد میں یا اس کا بر عکس ہو دونوں ممنوع میں ترتیب نہیں ہے نلهذا لا يجب الترتیب چونکہ ایتہ وضور میں اعضاء ار ہو کے ادا کرنے کے حکم میں واو عطف استعمال ہوتی ہے جو مطلق اجتماع کو چاہتی ہے اس لئے ترتیب واجب نہیں ہے ۔
دامانی السعی یہ جواب ہے ان حضرات کے استدلال کا جو واؤ کے برائے ترتیب ہونے کے لئے پیش کرتے تھے استدلال یوں ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا ان الصفا والمروة من شعائر اللہ تو صحابہ رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہم نے کہا باتما نبد ہم کس کے ساتھ ابتدا کریں تو نبی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا ابدد ایما بدا اللہ اسی کے ساتھ ابتدا کہ وجس کے ساتھ اللہ تعالیٰ نے ابتدا کی ہے تو نبی کریم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے اسی آیت سے ترتیب سمجھی اور اسی کا حکم دیا تو معلوم ہوا کہ واو برائے ترتیب ہے خلاصہ جواب یہ کہ سعی بین الصفا و المروۃ کے اندر جو ترتیب ہے وہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے قول ابدوا بما بدا اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ ہے۔ نہ کہ قرآن مجید کے ساتھ کیونکہ ان کا شعائر اللہ سے ہونا اس ترتیب کا متقاضی نہیں ہے ۔ وقولہ علیہ السلام ابد وا بما بدا الله تعالی بھی اس پر دال نہیں ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے جو ابتدا بالصفا فرمائی ہے۔ یہی تمہاری ہدایتہ کا موجب ہے لکن تقدیمہ لیکن جو صفا کو قرآن مجید میں مقد ما ذکر کیا گیا یہ کسی مصلحت سے خالی نہیں ہے مثلا تعظیم یا اس کی اہمیتہ وغیرہ ہو سکتی ہے اور یہ اہمیت اس کی اولویتہ کو تقاضا کرتی ہے نہ کہ وجوب کو البتہ وجوب ترتیب رسول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے لئے وحی غیر متلو سے ظاہر ہوئی اور ہمارے لئے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے قول ابدوا بما ابدا اللہ تعالیٰ سے بہر حال آیتہ جو مشتمل علی الود و العاطفہ ہے یہ موجب ترتیب نہیں ہے ۔ وزعم البعض انها للترتيب عند ابي حنيفة الخ بعض حضرات کا خیال ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک داو برائے ترتیب ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک برائے مقاز یقہ استدلالاً اپنے اس خیال پر بطور استدلال کے یہ مسئلہ پیش کرتے ہیں کہ جب ایک شخص اپنی بیوی غیر مدخولہ کو کہتا ہے
ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق وطالق وطالق تو امام کے نزدیک ایک طلاق اولی واقع ہوگی ثانیہ و ثالثہ واقع نہیں ہوگی تو معلوم ہوا کہ یہ معطوفات بالترتیب واقع ہو رہے ہیں سہیلی کے ساتھ عورت بائنہ ہو چکی ہے اس کے بعد ثانیہ اور ثالثہ کے لئے وہ عورت محل طلاق نہیں ہی اگر ترتیب کے لئے نہ ہوتی تو پھر یہ تینوں طلاق مجتمعا دفعة واحدة واقع ہوتیں تو وہ مطلقہ بالثلث ہو جاتی جیسے ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق ثلثا میں تین واقع ہو جاتی ہے اور یہاں صورة مسطورہ میں ایسے نہیں ہوا تو معلوم ہوا کہ وا و صرف جمعیتہ اور اجتماع کے لئے نہیں ہے بلکہ ترتیب کے لئے ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک تینوں واقع ہو جاتی ہیں تو ان کے نزدیک واد برا سے مقارنته و اجتماع ہوئی. وهذا اى زعم ذلك باطل یہ خیال اس بعض کا باطل ہے کہ و او ترتیب کے لئے ہے بلکہ اصل وجہ یہ ہے کہ امام اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اختلاف کی وجہ یہ ہے ان عندہ امامت کے نزدیک کیا تعلق الثاني والثالث یعنی جیسے طالق ثانی اور ثالث شرط کے ساتھ متعلق بواسطة الاول ہو رہا ہے وقوع بھی ایسے ہی ہوگا یعنی اولیٰ پہلے اور ثانی وثالث جو اس کے واسطہ سے معلق تھیں وہ بعد میں کیونکہ امام کے نزدیک جب ایک شرط کے ساتھ متعدد اجزیہ کو معلق کیا جائے تو ان کی تعلیق علی سبیل التعاقب ہوتی ہے۔ صورة مذکورہ میں جب کہا ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق یہ شرط و جزا مل کر جملہ کا ما مستقله ہو چکا ہے جو اپنے ما بعد کی طرف محتاج نہیں ہے اور تعلیق طلاق بالدخول حاصل ہو چکی ہے اور اس کے بعد جب کہا وطالق یہ تو جملہ ناقصہ ہے افادہ معنی میں یہ محتاج الی الجملة الاولی ہے تو اس کی تعلیق بعد تعلیق الا ولی حاصل ہوگی اور اسی طرح ثالثہ وانت طالق اس کی تعلیق بالشرط بعد تعلیق الثانیہ حاصل ہوگی تو جیسے تعلیق ہے وقوع بھی ایسے ہوگا فان المعلق بالشرط کا لمنجن عند الشرط یعنی جو طلاق معلق بالشرط ہوتی ہے تو عند الشرط یعنی بوقت تحقق شرط کے اس کا حکم منجز کا ہو جاتا ہے مثلاً تجیز غیر مدخولہ کو کہتا انت طالق وطالق وطالق تو اس صورت میں غیر مدخولہ پہلے لفظ طالق کے ساتھ بائنہ ہو جاتی اور ثانی وثالث لغو ہو جاتی کیونکہ عورت جب پہلی کے ساتھ بائنہ ہو چکی ہے تو ثانی و ثالث کے لئے وہ محل نہیں رہی اس لئے لغو ہو جائیں گی اور تعلیق بالشرط میں جب شرط متحقق ہو تو گویا کہ اس وقت اس نے کہا ہے انت طالق و طالق وطالق کیونکہ معلق بالشرط بوقت تحقق شرط تنجیز کی مانند ہو جاتی ہے نہ یہ کہ یہاں ترتیب والو کو دخل ہے اس کو کوئی دخل نہیں ہے لہذا استدلال صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ وعندهما يقع جملة اور صاحبین کے نزدیک یہ تینوں طالق وطالق وطالق جملہ واقع ہو جائینگی اور عورت مطلقہ بالثلاث ہو جائے گی لان الترتيب في التكلم کیونکہ یہ ترتیب طالق وطالق وطالق میں صرف تکلم اور بولنے میں ہے۔ لانی صيرورته نطلیقا اس کے طلاق بننے میں تو ترتیب نہیں ہے کیونکہ طلاق بننا تو ہوگا اس وقت جب شرط متحقق ہوگی اس وقت تو کوئی
They mention those to which there is severe need, and their name is 'huruf al-ma'ani'. Among them are the conjunctive letters. The waw is for absolute conjunction, i.e., it does not indicate order, but rather absolute collection. It is transmitted from the imams of language, and the induction of its places of usage also indicates that it is for absolute conjunction, and it is between two different nouns, i.e., it gives the same benefit between two different nouns as the alif of dual gives between two identical nouns. For example, if you say 'rajulan', similarly there will be a benefit in 'rajul wa imra'a', since the alif of dual is impossible between two different nouns, so the conjunctive waw is used. And their statement 'la ta'kul al-samaka wa tashrab al-labana' also has the same purpose: that you do not combine both, whether the fish first and the milk after or the opposite; both are prohibited, no order. Hence order is not obligatory. Because in the verse of wudu, when the command to perform the actions of the limbs uses the conjunctive waw, which demands absolute combination, therefore order is not obligatory.
This is the answer to the argument of those who present that waw is for order. The argument is that Allah said 'Indeed al-Safa and al-Marwa are among the symbols of Allah.' The Companions said, 'With which shall we begin?' So the Prophet said, 'Begin with what Allah began.' So the Prophet understood order from this verse and ordered it, so it is known that waw is for order. The summary of the answer is that the order in sa'yi between Safa and Marwa is from the Prophet's statement 'Begin with what Allah began', not from the Qur'an, because their being from the symbols of Allah does not necessitate that order. And his statement 'Begin with what Allah began' does not indicate that Allah began with Safa. That is the cause of your guidance, but its precedence in the Qur'an is not devoid of some wisdom, like glorification or its importance, and this importance necessitates its priority, not obligation. Rather, the obligation of order was revealed to the Prophet through unrecited revelation (wahy ghayr matlu), and for us it is from the Prophet's statement 'Begin with what Allah began'. In any case, the verse which contains the conjunctive waw does not necessitate order.
And some claim that waw is for order according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the Sahibayn it is for concurrence. They argue on this claim by presenting the case when a man says to his non-consummated wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced.' According to Abu Hanifa, only the first divorce occurs, the second and third do not occur. So it is known that the conjuncts occur in order. Because after the first, the woman becomes separated (ba'in), and she is no longer a locus for divorce for the second and third. If waw were not for order, then all three divorces would occur together at once, and she would be triple-divorced, like if he said 'if you enter the house, you are triple divorced' where three occur. And here in the stated case it is not so, so it is known that waw is not for mere conjunction and collection, but for order. And according to the Sahibayn, all three occur, so for them waw indicates concurrence and combination.
And this claim is false. Rather, the real reason for the difference between Imam and the Sahibayn is that according to Imam, the relation of the second and third (divorce) to the condition is through the first (i.e., via the first), so occurrence will be similarly: the first first, and the second and third which were suspended through it will occur later. Because according to Imam, when multiple parts are suspended on one condition, their suspension is in a sequential manner. In the stated case, when he said 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', this condition and consequence have become a complete independent sentence, not needing what follows. And the suspension of divorce on entry is achieved. Then when he said 'and divorced', this is an incomplete sentence, needing the first sentence for meaning. So its suspension is after the first suspension. Similarly for the third 'and you are divorced', its suspension on the condition is after the suspension of the second. So as the suspension is, so is occurrence. Because the suspended upon condition becomes like an immediate pronouncement at the occurrence of the condition. For example, in an immediate pronouncement, if he says to a non-consummated wife 'you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced', the non-consummated wife becomes separated with the first word 'divorced', and the second and third become void because the woman, having become separated, is no longer a locus for divorce. And in conditional suspension, when the condition is realized, it is as if he said then 'you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced', because a conditional suspender at the time of the condition's realization becomes like an immediate pronouncement. It is not that the waw for order is involved; it has no role. Therefore the argument is not correct.
And according to the Sahibayn, all three 'divorced, and divorced, and divorced' occur together and the woman becomes triple-divorced. Because the order is only in speech; its becoming divorce has no order, because divorce occurs only at the moment the condition is realized, and at that moment there is no order.
